PREFACE
This text offers a substantial treatment of plane and spherical 
trigonometry incorporating current collegiate viewpoints in the content, 
arrangement, and emphasis. The book is designed for a college freshman 
who is likely to use trigonometry in more advanced mathematics and/or 
college courses in other fields. The principal parts of the text are couched 
at a level which can be attained by a student who has a satisfactory 
foundation in intermediate algebra. Supplementary material is available 
to test the abilities of interested students with more substantial previous 
mathematics. The book commences with an introductory chapter on 
the trigonometry of the general angle. The sections on analytic trigo­
nometry are definitely emphasized, and are oriented with respect to 
needs in later mathematics. The treatment of computation, logarithms, 
and numerical trigonometry is well rounded, but is not overemphasized. 
Applications of plane triangles are suitably numerous, with a modern 
flavor. If desired, a relatively brief course in plane trigonometry can 
be organized conveniently on the basis of this text by merely omitting 
various whole chapters, or their final sections. The content includes a 
foundation in spherical trigonometry, with a chapter on its elementary 
applications. The text is bound with the author’s extensive Logarithmic 
and Trigonometric Tables, augmented by a new six-page table of haver­
sines and their logarithms.
The following features of the text may be noted.
Considerable care about terminology and definitions.
Specific measures to attain simplicity, clarity, and a high mathematical 
standard in the proofs and explanations.
Large numbers of illustrative examples.
Abundant problem material, with the examples carefully graded.
Numerous miscellaneous review exercises, including a very large com­
prehensive review list at the end of the Plane Trigonometry.
Special attention to questions about the accuracy of computation.
v

I
vi 
PREFACE
An expansive treatment of trigonometric equations and identities, 
arranged to allow various stages in the emphasis on this content.
Interpolation to tenths of a minute with five-place logarithms of the 
trigonometric functions.
A flexible organization of the Spherical Trigonometry which permits a 
very brief treatment, including the main applications, if a complete 
discussion of the content is not desired.
Arrangements which allow emphasis on either four-place or five-place 
logarithmic computation.
Division of the treatment of graphs between two chapters, to gain the 
advantage of a double exposure as well as to delay introduction of 
the more advanced aspects.
Answers for the odd-numbered problems bound with the text, and 
answers for the even-numbered problems in a separate pamphlet, 
when requested by the teacher.
Considerable use of haversines in the Spherical Trigonometry, with an 
associated five-place table of haversines and their logarithms.
Inclusion of a five-place table of the natural values of secants and co­
secants.
Ample provision for supplementary work, where the material is ear­
marked with a black star or occurs at the ends of chapters or exercises.

PLANE TRIGONOMETRY


ONE
FUNCTIONS OF THE GENERAL ANGLE
1. Introduction
In the history of mathematics, we find that the subject called trigo­
nometry was first developed because of its usefulness in the indirect 
measurement of angles and distances, particularly in surveying and 
astronomy. Trigonometry is an essential tool in engineering and the 
physical sciences. The theoretical aspects of trigonometry are indispen­
sable in pure mathematics and also in many fields of application.
2. Real numbers
The numbers met in arithmetic and the most elementary parts of 
later mathematics are called real numbers. They are classed as posi­
tive, negative, or zero. All numbers to be met in this text will be real 
numbers, except in Chapter Eleven. Any single letter which we introduce 
without a qualifying description will represent a number. For contrast 
with explicit numbers like 3, — 5, etc., we may call number symbols such 
as a, b, x, and y literal numbers.
The reciprocal of a number N is defined as 1/N. Thus, the reciprocal 
of a fraction a/b is b/a because

Illustration 1. The reciprocal of 5 is 1/5; of 5/7 is 7/5.
3. Absolute value
The absolute value, or numerical value, of a positive number or zero is 
defined as the number itself. The absolute value of a negative number 
is the negative of the given number. The absolute value of a number N 
is frequently represented by the symbol | N |. We say that two numbers 
b and c are numerically equal if b and c have the same absolute value. 
Then, b and c differ at most in sign.
Illustration 1. The absolute value of + 5 is + 5. The absolute value of 
— 5 also is + 5. We read | — 3 j as “the absolute value of — 3 ”; | — 3 | = 3.

6. Inequality
One number M is said to be less than a second number N, or N is 
greater than M, in case M is to the left of N on the number scale, as in 
Figure 2. We use the inequality signs < and > to represent less than 
and greater than, respectively. On considering various relative positions 
for M and N, we verify that the statement “M < N” is equivalent to 
saying that (2V — M) is positive.

5
Illustration 1. — 6 < — 2 because — 6 is to the left of — 2 on the number 
scale; also, we note that - 2- (-6) = -2 + 6 = 4, which is positive. Simi­
larly, — 4 < 2; 0 < 8; — 3 < 0. To state that M is positive is equivalent to 
saying M > 0. To state that M is negative is equivalent to saying M < 0.
We say that one number b is numerically less than a second number c 
in case the absolute value of b is less than the absolute value of c, or | b | < | c |. 
For contrast with numerical inequality, we sometimes place the word 
algebraically before greater than or less than as used in the ordinary sense.
Illustration 2. — 3 is algebraically greater than — 7, or — 3 > — 7, but 
— 3 is numerically less than — 7 because
| - 3 | = 3, 
| - 7 | = 7, and 3 < 7.
7. Rectangular coordinates
On each of the perpendicular lines OX and OY in Figure 3, we lay off 
a scale with 0 as the zero point. In the plane of OX and OY, we shall 
measure distances vertically in terms of the unit on OY, and horizontally 
in terms of the unit on OX. Also, we agree that all horizontal and vertical 
distances or line segments will be directed, with the positive direction 
upward on vertical lines and to the right on horizontal lines.
Let P be any point in the plane of OX and OY. Then, by definition, 
the horizontal coordinate, or the abscissa of P, is the directed perpendicu­
lar distance x from OY to P, where x is positive if P is to the right of OF 
and negative if P is to the left of 0 Y. The vertical coordinate, or the ordinate 
of P, is the directed perpendicular dis­
tance y from OX to P, where y is positive 
if P is above OX and negative if P is below 
OX. Each of the lines OX and OY is 
called a coordinate axis, and their inter­
section 0 is called the origin of coordi­
nates. The abscissa and ordinate of P 
together are called the rectangular co­
ordinates of P. We shall use “ P: (x, y)” 
to abbreviate “point P with coordinates 
x, y.” With axes labeled OX and OY, 
we may refer to the abscissa as the x- 
coordinate, and to the ordinate as the y- 
coordinate. The plane of OX and OF is 
called the coordinate plane.
Illustration 1. In Figure 3, R has the coordinates (x = — 3, y = 4), or R 
is the point (— 3, 4). We read 7?:(— 3, 4) as “R, — 3, 4.”
Illustration 2. In the coordinate plane, the locus of all points with the 
abscissa 4 is the line perpendicular to OX where x = 4. We say that the equation 
of this line is x = 4.

FUNCTIONS OF THE GENERAL ANGLE

8. Directed angles
In elementary geometry, an angle was thought of merely as a ready­
made figure consisting of two half-lines radiating from a common point, 
and the measure of an angle was always a positive number or zero. For 
many reasons, it proves convenient to generalize our previous notion of 
an angle as follows.
Suppose that a half-line or ray, issuing from a fixed point 0, rotates 
about 0 either clockwise or counter-clockwise from an initial position OA 
to a terminal position OB, as in Figure 4. Then, this rotation is said to 
generate an angle AOB whose initial side is OA and terminal side is OB. 
As a unit for measuring the amount of rotation, we define 1° as 1/360 
of a complete revolution. Then, the value of Z.A0B is defined as the 
amount of rotation used in generating the angle, where we agree to 
consider counter-clockwise rotation as positive, and clockwise rotation 
as negative. The description of an angle is incomplete until we are told 
the direction and amount of rotation used in forming the angle. In a figure, 
we may show the amount of rotation by a curved arrow.
Illustration 1. In Figure 4, the configuration AOB is the same in each 
diagram. In (1), Z.AOB — 45°. In (2), a complete revolution is indicated be­
sides 45°: Z.AOB = 360° -|- 45° = 405°. In (3), the rotation is 45° less than 
360°, clockwise: Z.AOB = — 315°. In (4), LBOA ==■ — 45°.
Fig. 4
A'oZe 7. The angular unit 1° is subdivided as in elementary geometry. Thus, 
we define one minute, 1', as 1/60 of 1°, and one second, 1", as 1/60 of 1'. The re­
sulting system involving degrees, minutes, and seconds is called the sexagesimal 
system of angular measurement. The name sexagesimal is related to the Latin 
word sexagesimus, meaning sixtieth. Other systems of angular measurement will 
be introduced later.
Illustration 2. Any positive angle whose value lies between 0° and 90° is 
called an acute angle. Any positive angle between 90° and 180° is called an 
obtuse angle.
Any number of complete revolutions, clockwise or counter-clockwise, 
may be added to any of the rotations in Figure 4 without altering the 
initial and terminal sides of the angles. Thus, we see that infinitely many 
positive and negative angles of unlimited numerical values correspond 
to any given pair of sides for the angles.

10. The function concept
In a given problem, a constant is a number symbol whose value does 
not change during the discussion. A variable is a number symbol which 
may take on different values, called the range of the variable. When 
desirable, we may think of a constant as a variable which may assume 
just one value. Related variables arise frequently, and thus we meet 
the following basic terminology.
Definition I. If a first variable u and a second variable v are so related 
that, whenever a value is assigned to u, a corresponding value or corre­
sponding values of v can be determined, it is said that v is a function 
of u. In such a case, u is called the independent variable and v the de­
pendent variable.
Illustration 1, The area A of a circle is given by A = irr2, where r is the 
radius. In considering different circles, tt is a constant while r and A are variables, 
and A is a function of r.

Example 2. If sin 0 > 0 and cot 0 < 0, in what quadrant does 0 lie?
Solution. Since sin 0 > 0, 0 is in either quadrant I or quadrant II. Since 
cot 0 < 0, 0 is in either quadrant II or quadrant IV. Hence, 0 must lie in quad­
rant II.

FUNCTIONS OF THE GENERAL ANGLE
We have seen that we may compute the exact values of the trigo­
nometric functions of quadrantal angles, and of any angle having 30°, 
45°, or 60° as the reference angle. The functions of any angle may be 
computed by methods discussed in advanced mathematics. The func­
tions of selected acute angles are collected in tables, to be considered 
later. The functions of any angle 9 will then be found by attaching the 
proper signs to the functions of a corresponding reference angle a, whose 
functions will be obtained from tables.

The trigonometric functions may be grouped as follows: sine and co-
sine; tangent and cotangent; secant and cosecant. In each pair, either 
function may be referred to as the cofunction of the other one. Then, 
(1) states that any function of an acute angle a equals the cofunction of the 
complement of a. We call (1) the cofunction relations.
Illustration 2. sin 33° = cos 57°. tan 29° = cot 61°. esc 15° = sec 75°.
Example 1. Express tan 37° 38' as a function of another acute angle.
Solution. Since 90° - 37° 38' = 52° 22', tan 37° 38' = cot 52° 22'.
Note 1. In the Middle Ages, it was customary at first to refer to the cosine, 
in Latin, as complementi sinus, meaning the sine of the complement. Eventually, 
complementi sinus was abbreviated to cosinus.

20. Significant digits
Unless otherwise indicated, let us assume hereafter that any number 
referred to is written in decimal form. Then, in any number, we may 
visualize an endless sequence of zeros at the right of the last digit which 
is not zero, if there is such a last digit. A terminating decimal is one in 
which all digits to the right of a certain place are zeros. An endless decimal 
4s one in which, no matter how far we proceed to the right, we never 
reach a place beyond which each digit is zero.
Illustration 1. If = 1.75000 • • •, a terminating decimal. | = .333 • • •, 
an endless repeating decimal, tt = 3.14159 • • •, an endless but not a repeating 
decimal.
In any number N, let us read its digits from left to right. Then, by 
definition, the significant digits, or significant figures, of N are its digits, 
in sequence, commencing with the first digit not zero and ending with the 
last one definitely specified. Notice that this definition does not involve 
any reference to the position of the decimal point in N. Usually, we 
mention any final zeros at the right in referring to the significant digits 
of N only when N is an approximate value of some quantity.
Illustration 2. The significant digits of 410.58 or of .0041058 are 4, 1, 0, 
5, 8. Since 21f is exactly 21.8, we would say that it has three significant digits, 
even though we could add zeros.

22
FUNCTIONS OF ACUTE ANGLES
If T is the true value and A is an approximate value of a quantity, we 
agree to call the absolute value of T — A the error of A. Any approximate 
value A should be written so that its error is at most one half unit in the 
last significant place used in A. If A has just k significant digits, A is 
called a fe-place approximation to T; then, the error of A is at most one 
half unit in the fcth significant place in A, or not more than 5 units in the 
next place to the right.
Illustration 3. If a surveyor specifies that a measured distance is 256.8 
yards, he should mean that the error is at most .05 yard and, thus, that the true 
result lies between 256.75 and 256.85, inclusive.
Illustration 4. It is essential to specify any final zeros in an approximate 
value A. Thus, to say that a measured weight is 35.60 (pounds) means that the 
true weight lies between 35.595 and 35.605. To say that the measured weight is 
35.6 means that the true weight lies between 35.55 and 35.65. Thus, there is a 
distinction between 35.60 and 35.6 as approximate values.
Note 1. In referring to a place in a number, we shall mean any place where 
a significant digit stands. In referring to a decimal place, the word decimal will 
be explicitly used.
To round off a number N to k figures, as the student has done in arith­
metic,
*
 means to write a fc-place approximation A for N.
* For a review of some rules of arithmetic for computation with approximation 
values, see page 164 in the Appendix.
Illustration 5. To round off ?r = 3.141593 to five places, we write 3.1416; 
we increased the 5th significant digit of tt from 5 to 6 because the discarded part 
.000093 is greater than 5 in the 5th decimal place. The three-place approximation 
to ir is 3.14, where we kept 4 unaltered because the discarded part is less than 5 
in the third decimal place.
Illustration 6. In rounding off 315.475 to five places, with equal justifica­
tion we could give either 315.48 or 315.47. Arbitrarily, in such an ambiguous case, 
in this book we agree to write the result with the last digit even, and hence 
choose 315.48.
Until otherwise stated in this text, we shall assume that the data in 
any problem are exact, and we shall compute our results as accurately 
as is justified by the means at our disposal. Later, we shall introduce 
agreements about approximate data.
21. Four-place trigonometric table
In general, any value of a trigonometric function is an endless decimal. 
By advanced methods, the functions of any angle can be computed to 
as many decimal places as desired. We shall learn later how the functions 
of any angle 0 can be expressed in terms of the functions of a reference 
angle a for 0, where 0° £ a £ 90°. Hence, if a table is available for

23
finding functions of angles from 0° to 90°, inclusive, the table will serve 
indirectly for corresponding angles of any size.
Table VII is a four-place table of the functions of acute angles at 
intervals of 10'. For angles at most equal to 45°, read angles at the left 
and titles of columns at the top in the main part of each page of the table. 
For angles greater than 45°, read titles at the bottom and angles at the 
right.
Illustration 1. To find cot 5° 30', look in the left-hand angle columns for 
5° 30': cot 5° 30' = 10.39. To find sin 77° 20', look for 77° 20' in the right-hand 
angle columns: sin 77° 20' = .9757.
Each entry in the function columns of Table VII is a function of some 
angle and, also, is the cofunction of the complementary angle, on account 
of the relations of Section 18 on page 20.
Illustration 2. We read in Table VII that
.9757 = sin 77° 20' = cos 12° 40'.

.4173 on page 15 in the column with cosine at the bottom; hence we read
.4173 = cos 65° 20', or a = 65° 20'.
Notel. One of the most famous of the early printed tables of the trigonometric

Rhaeticus and published in 1596. This table gives the values of trigonometric 
functions to ten decimal places for angles spaced at intervals of 10".

22. Interpolation in a four-place table
With the assistance of a method called interpolation by proportional 
parts, we shall use Table VII for angles not tabulated there. In this 
method, it is assumed that, for small changes in an angle a, the correspond­
ing changes in the value of any one of the functions of a are proportional to 
the changes in a. This assumption, which is only approximately true, 
is called the principle of proportional parts. The principle leads to results 
which are sufficiently accurate for practical purposes.
Example 1. By use of Table VII, find sin 27° 43'.
Solution. 1. Notice that 27° 43' is 3/10 of the way from 27° 40' to 27° 50'.
2. By the principle of proportional parts, an increase of 3' in the angle 
27° 40' causes 3/10 as much change in its sine as is caused by an increase of 10' 
in the angle. Since 27° 43' is 3/10 of the way from 27° 40' to 27° 50', sin 27° 43' 
is 3/10 of the way from sin 27° 40' to sin 27° 50'. Therefore,
sin 27° 43' = sin 27° 40' + .3(sin 27° 50' - sin 27° 40').
From table: sin 27° 40' = .4643" 
sin 27° 43' = ?
26
Tabular difference is 
.4669 - .4643 = .0026.
From table: sin 27° 50' = .4669J
,3(.0026) = .00078, or .0008.
3. 
Hence, sin 27° 43' = .4643 + .0008 = .4651.
Comment. 1. We call .0008 the proportional part of the tabular difference. 
In passing from .4643 to .4669 there is an increase of .0026; hence we added .0008.
2. We changed from .00078 to .0008 in accordance with Rule I on page 164, 
because the table entries are accurate only to four decimal places.
Example 2. Find esc 19° 27' from Table VII.
Solution. 1. 19° 27' is 7/10 of the way from 19° 20' to 19° 30'.
From table: esc 19° 20' = 3.021"
CSC 19° 27' =

.025

3.021 - 2.996 = .025.
From table: esc 19° 30' = 2.996J
.7(25) = 17.5, or 17.
2. Hence, esc 19° 27' = 3.021 - .017 = 3.004.
Comment. 1. We subtracted .017 because 3.021 > 2.996.
2. We found .7(25) = 17.5 under the tenths of 25 in the column of Propor­
tional Parts in the table. We could use 17.5 as either 17 or 18 with equal justifica­
tion. In interpolation, whenever such an ambiguity is met, we agree to choose the 
last digit so as to make the final result even in the last place.
3. For a different solution, we could observe that 19° 27' is 3/10 of the way 
from 19° 30' to 19° 20'. Then, since esc 19° 20' is greater than esc 19° 30', we 
would add 3/10 of the tabular difference to obtain esc 19° 27':
esc 19° 27' = 2.996 + .3(.O25) = 2.996 + .0075 = 3.004.

23. Introduction to inverse trigonometric functions
The expression “arcsin ft,” which we read “arcsine h,” means 
“an angle whose sine is h.”
(1)

26 
FUNCTIONS OF ACUTE ANGLES
It is sometimes instructive to read arcsin h without abbreviation, as indi­
cated in (1). By definition, the following equations are equivalent:
h = sin a and a = arcsin h. 
(2)
If we think of a as a variable, the equation h = sin a describes h as a 
function of a; the equation a = arcsin h serves the inverse purpose of 
describing a as a function of h. Hence, we refer to arcsin h as an inverse 
trigonometric function of h. Similarly, we introduce the other inverse 
functions with meanings corresponding to (1):
arccos h; arctan h; arccot h; arcsec h; arccsc h.
Illustration 1. Since tan 45° = 1, then arctan 1 = 45°, which we read 
“arctangent 1 equals 45°.”
Example 1. Find arccos .3287.
Solution. We could read the problem as follows: “find an angle whose cosine 
is .3287,” or “find arccosine .3287.” Let a = arccos .3287. Then cos a = .3287. 
By interpolation in Table VII, we find a = 70° 49'. That is,
arccos .3287 = 70° 49'.
At present, any inverse trigonometric function is taken to represent 
an acute angle. Later, we shall observe that an inverse function, such 
as arcsin h, where | h | g 1, has infinitely many values.

24. Five-place values of the functions
By means of Table XI, without interpolation we can find the func­
tions of an acute angle a to five-decimal places if a is expressed to the 
nearest minute. And, if a function of a is given, without interpolation 
we can find a to the nearest minute by use of Table XI. By interpola­
tion in Table XI, we can find the functions of angles expressed to the 
nearest tenth of a minute. And, if the five-place value of a function of 
a is given, we can find a to tenths of a minute.
Illustration 1. From page 96 in Table XI, sin 67° 27' = .92355. If 
sin a = .57071, from Table XI we find a = 34° 48'.
Illustration 2. If sin a = .03915, in the sine column for 2° in Table XI, 
the nearest entry is .03926. Hence, a = 2° 15', to the nearest minute.

A right triangle has six parts, consisting of three 
sides and three angles, one of which is 90°. By means 
of trigonometry, if two sides or an acute angle and a 
side of a right triangle are given,

cent to a equals the hypotenuse times cos a. We shall avoid (1) until we

useful in avoiding division when finding c.

Summary. Suggestions for solving a triangle.
1. By use of the data, sketch the triangle roughly to scale.
2. To find any unknown part, use a formula involving it but no other 
unknown. If possible, select a formula avoiding division.
3. As a rough check, compare results with the preliminary sketch. 
For a more refined check, substitute values in any one of formulas 
(3) to (8) which was not used in solving but which involves as many 
as possible of the computed parts; after substituting, compare the two 
sides of the check formula.
4. In the arithmetic, round off all lengths, quotients, and products to 
four or to five significant digits, and all angles to minutes or to tenths of 
a minute, according as four-place or five-place tables are used.

26. Applications of right triangles
In any geometrical application of trigonometry, it is advisable to 
perform the following actions before computing.
1. Construct a figure roughly to scale for the data.
2. Introduce single letters to represent unknown angles or lengths.
3. Outline the solution by specifying triangles or formulas to use; solve 
each formula for the quantity to be obtained from it.
In either diagram in Figure 29, O is a point from which we sight an 
object at C, and OH is a horizontal line in the same vertical plane as C. 
Then, the acute angle COH between the line of sight to C and the hori­
zontal line is called the angle of elevation of C or the angle of depression 
of C, as seen from O, according as C is above O or below O.

FUNCTIONS OF ACUTE ANGLES

27. 
Simplification of computation
We shall define certain auxiliary numbers called logarithms. These 
will permit us to change the operations of multiplication, division, raising 
to a power, and extraction of a root to the easier operations of addition, 
subtraction, multiplication, and division, respectively, applied to the 
proper logarithms.
28. 
Rational and irrational numbers
A rational number is a number which can be expressed as a fraction 
u/v, where u and v are integers. A real number which is not a rational 
number is called an irrational number.
Illustration 1. 4, — 5, 0, and V are rational numbers, it, V% and 
are irrational numbers.

We shall use irrational numbers as exponents, but a logical founda­

the preceding index laws hold if the exponents are any real numbers, 
when the base is positive.
30. 
Logarithms
Let b represent a fixed positive number, not 1. Then, if N is any 
positive number, there exists a corresponding real number x such that 
N = bx. This leads to the following definition.
Definition I. The logarithm of a number N with respect to the base b
is the exponent of the power to which b must be raised to obtain N.
In other words, if N = bx then x is the logarithm of N to the base b.
To abbreviate “the logarithm of N to the base b," we write “logbW.” 
Then, by Definition I, the following equations state the same fact, the 
first equation in exponential form and the second in logarithmic form:
N = bx and x = log& N. 
(1)
Illustration 1. If .V = 45, then 5 is the logarithm of N to the base 4.
We read “log; 64” as “the logarithm of 64 to the base 2.”
Since 64 = 26, log2 64 = 6.

If b > 0 and x is real, then bx is positive in (1). This shows why we 
restrict Definition I to the case where N is positive. Thus, we shall not 
define or use logarithms of negative numbers. In very advanced mathe­
matics, if N < 0 or b < 0, then logt N is defined as a complex number.

Properties of logarithms useful in computation----------------------------------------
I. The logarithm of a product equals the sum of the logarithms of its 
factors. For instance,
logb MN = logb M + logb N. 
(1)
Illustration 1. 
logic 897(596) = logic 897 + logw 596.

M = bx and N = by. (Definition of a logarithm)
MN = bxbv = bx+v. 
(A law of exponents)
Hence, by the definition of a logarithm,
logb MN = x + y = logb M + logb N.
Note 1. By use of (1), we may prove Property I for a product of any number 
°f factors. Thus, since MNP =

Common logarithms________________________________________________
Logarithms to the base 10 are called common logarithms, and are the 
most useful variety for computational purposes. Hereafter, unless other­
wise stated, a logarithm will mean a common logarithm. For abbreviation, 
we use merely log N instead of logw N for the common logarithm of N.

33. Characteristic and mantissa
Every real number, and hence every logarithm, can be written as 
the sum of an integer and a decimal fraction, which is positive or zero and 
less than 1. After log N is written in this way, we call the integer the 
characteristic and the fraction the mantissa of log N:
log N = (integer) + (positive or zero fraction, < 1); 
log N = characteristic + mantissa. J
Since the characteristic is an integer and the mantissa is less than 1, 
the characteristic is negative if and only if log N is negative.
Illustration 1. Log N is an integer if and only if N is an integral power 
of 10. Thus, log 1000 = 3; in this case the mantissa is zero and the character­
istic is 3.
(1)
Illustration 2. If log N = 4.6832 = 4 + .6832, the characteristic is 4 
and the mantissa is .6832.
Illustration 3. If log .V = — 3.75, log A7 lies between — 4 and — 3. 
Hence, log N = — 4 + (a fraction). To find this fraction we obtain 4 — 3.75, 
or .25. Hence, log N = — 3.75 = — 4 + .25, and the characteristic is — 4 
while the mantissa is .25.----------------------------------------------------------

34. Properties of the characteristic and the mantissa
Illustration 1. All numbers whose logarithms are given below have the 
same significant digits, (3, 8, 0, 4). To obtain the logarithms, log 3.804 was 
first found from a table to be discussed later; the other logarithms were then 
obtained by use of Properties I and II of Section 31.
log 380.4 
= log 100(3.804) = log 100 + log 3.804 = 
2 + .5802;
log 38.04 
= log 10(3.804) = log 10 + log 3.804 
=

log 3.804 = .5802 
= 
0 + .5802;
log .3804 
= log 
= log 3.804 - log 10 = - 1 + .5802;
log .03804 = log 
= log 3.804 - log 100 = - 2 + .5802.
Similarly, if N is any number whose significant digits are (3, 8, 0, 4), then N 
is equal to 3.804 multiplied, or else divided, by an integral power of 10; hence, 
it follows as above that .5802 is the mantissa of log N.
In Illustration 1 we met special cases of the following theorems.
I. The mantissa of log N depends only on the sequence of significant 
digits in N. That is, if two numbers differ only in the position of the 
decimal point, their logarithms have the same mantissa.
II. TF/zen, N > 1, the characteristic of log Ar is an integer, positive or 
zero, which is one less than the number of digits in N to the left of 
the decimal point.
III. If N < 1, the characteristic of log N is a negative integer; if the 
first significant digit of N is in the Ath decimal place, then — A is the 
characteristic of log N.
Illustration 2. We find the characteristic of log N by merely inspecting 
N. Thus, by Theorem III, the characteristic of log .00039 is -4 because 3 is in 
the 4th decimal place. By Theorem II, the characteristic of log 15,786 is 4, 
because there are five digits to the left of the decimal point (understood after 6).
Note 1. Besides common logarithms, the only other variety used appre­
ciably is the system of natural logarithms, for which the base is a certain 
irrational number denoted by e where e = 2.71828 • • •.
Note 2. Logarithms were invented by a Scotchman, John Napier, Baron 
of Merchiston (1550-1617). His original logarithms were not defined as ex­
ponents of powers of a base. Common logarithms were invented by an English­
man, Henry Briggs (1556-1631), who was aided by Napier, Common 
logarithms are sometimes called Briggs logarithms.

36. Tables of logarithms
Mantissas can be computed by advanced methods and, except in 
special cases, are endless decimal fractions. Computed mantissas are 
found in tables of logarithms, also called tables of mantissas.
Illustration 1. The mantissa for log 10705 is .029586671630457, to 
fifteen decimal places.
Table VIII gives the mantissa of log N correct to five decimal places 
if N is any number with at most four significant digits aside from addi­
tional zeros at the right. If N lies between 1 and 10, the characteristic 
of log N is zero so that log N is the same as its mantissa. Hence, a five- 
place table of mantissas is also a table of the actual logarithms of all 
numbers with at most four significant digits, from N = 1.000 to N = 9.999. 
In case N is less than 1 or greater than 10, we must supply the charac­
teristic of log N by use of Theorems II and III of Section 34, besides ob­
taining the mantissa of log N from Table VIII.

37. Interpolation in a table of logarithms
Interpolation in a table of mantissas is based on the assumption that, 
for small changes in N, the corresponding changes in log N are proportional 
to the changes in N. This principle of proportional parts is merely an 
approximation to the truth but is sufficiently accurate for our purposes.
We agree that, when a mantissa is found by interpolation from a 
table, we shall express the result only to the number of decimal places given 
in table entries. Also, in finding N by interpolation in a table of mantissas 
when log N is given, we agree to specify just four or just five significant 
digits according as we are using a four-place or a five-place table. No 
greater refinement is justified.
In using a five-place table of logarithms, it is convenient to act as 
if each number N whose logarithm is to be mentioned has just five signifi­
cant digits, perhaps including one or more zeros at the right. Thus, we 
attach one or more final zeros to the significant part of N if it has less 
than five significant digits. Or, we round off N to five significant digits 
>f N initially has more than five-place accuracy. We then think of each 
entry in Table VIII as the mantissa for the logarithm of a number N 
with five significant digits, obtained by attaching a final zero to the 
significant part arising in the table.
* No interpolation is called for in this exercise.

38. The scientific notation for numbers
We sometimes write a number N as the product of an integral power 
of 10 and a number P equal to or greater than 1 but less than 10, where 
P has the same significant digits as N. The resulting expression, 
N= P(10
*),
 is called the scientific notation for N, and is convenient in 
abbreviating very large or very small numbers, and in indicating how 
many digits are significant in approximate values.
Illustration 1. .000’000’368 = 3.68(.000’000’l) = 3.68(10-7).
.000368 = 3.68(.0001) = 3.68(10 4)-
132,560,000 = 1.3256(100,000,000) = 1.3256(108).
Illustration 2. If 5,630,000 is an approximate value in which five digits 
are significant, we write 5.6300(106) for the number.
If N — P(10fc) where 1 is P < 10, then 0 log P < 1 and
log N = log P + log IO41 = k + log P, 
or k is the characteristic and log P is the mantissa of log N.

This form shows explicitly that only four digits are significant in N.

39. Computation of products and quotients
Unless otherwise specified, we continue to assume that the data of 
any problem are exact. Then, the accuracy of a product, quotient,, or 
power computed by use of logarithms depends on the number of places 
in the table being used. The result is frequently subject to an unavoid­
able error which, as a rule, is at most a few units in the last significant 
place given by interpolation. Hence, we compute with at least five-place 
logarithms to obtain four-place accuracy, and with at least four-place 
logarithms to obtain three-place accuracy. As a general custom, in any 
result we shall give all digits obtainable by interpolation in the specified 
table.

42. Problems in computation
It is important to realize that the properties of logarithms offer no 
assistance in the computation of a sum or difference, except perhaps in 
the auxiliary computation of individual terms.

*43. Logarithmic and exponential equations
A logarithmic equation is one where the logarithm of some expression 
involving an unknown is present.
------- --------------------- --------------------- -------------- --------- ______________
Example 1. Solve for x: 
log z + log = 6.
Solution. By use of Properties I and II of Section 31, 
log x + (log 2 + log x — log 5) = 6.
2 log x = 6 + log 5 - log 2 = 6.3980. 
(Table V)
log x = 3.1990; x = antilog 3.1990 = 1581. 
(Table V)
An equation where an unknown appears in an exponent is called an 
exponential equation. Sometimes, such an equation can be solved by 
taking the logarithm of each member to eliminate exponents.

The number logo b is called the modulus of the system of base a

*45. Graphs of exponential and logarithmic functions
Since the equations y = logs x and x = by are equivalent, they have 
the same graph.
Illustration 1. Figure 31 is a graph of y = logs x when b = 2.71828 • ■ ■ 
but the general characteristics of the graph would not be changed if b had any 
other value greater than 1. This graph emphasizes the following facts:
1. If 0 < x < 1, logs x < 0, and logs x > 0 if x >1.
2. 
For any b > 0, logs 1=0.
3. If x increases without bound, logs, x increases without bound.
4. If x approaches zero as a limit, logs x decreases without bound, or becomes 
negatively infinite.

46. Logarithms of trigonometric functions
Example 1. Compute 156 sin 21° 10' by four-place logarithms.
Solution. In Table VI we find log sin 21° 10', the logarithm of sin 21° 10'. 
We read “log sin 21° 10'” briefly as it is written: “log, sine, 21° 10'.”
log 156 = 2.1931 
(Table V)
(+) log sin 21° 10' = 9.5576 - 10 
(Table VI)
log 156 sin 21° 10' = 1.7507. Hence, 156 sin 21° 10' = 56.32,
where we found antilog 1.7507 from Table V.
Comment. 1. The sine or cosine of any angle between 0° and 90°, or the 
tangent of any angle between 0° and 45°, or the cotangent of any angle between 
45° and 90° is not zero and is less than 1. Therefore the logarithms of these 
functions have negative characteristics. For abbreviation, Tables VI and IX 
omit “ — 10” belonging with each of these logarithms which is tabulated. 
Hence, in using the columns labeled log sin, log cos, and log tan for 0° to 45°, 
we subtract 10 from each given entry.
2. Table VI made it unnecessary to use Table VII in Example 1.
To abbreviate the tables, we omit the logarithms of secants and 
cosecants. Hence, before computing a trigonometric expression by 
use of logarithms, if a secant or cosecant occurs as a factor, we change 
this function to the reciprocal of the cosine or sine, respectively.
Note 1. Recall that tan a = 1/cot a and hence
log tan a = log 1 — log cot a = 0 — log cot a = — log cot a.
Therefore, any change in log tan a corresponds to a change of equal numerical 
value, but opposite sign, in log cot a. Hence, in Table IX, the single column 
headed “cd” gives the common differences for the columns headed log tan and 
log cot.
Sometimes the values of trigonometric functions are referred to as 
natural functions, to emphasize the distinction between them and 
their logarithms. We call Tables VII and XI tables of the natural trigono­
metric functions.

lying between 0° and 90°, when trigonometric functions are mentioned.

47. Accuracy of certain results
If the logarithm of some function of an unknown angle a is given, we 
find a by use of the four-place Table VI or /fee-place Table IX accord­
ing as the given logarithm is specified to four or to five decimal places, 
respectively. Then, we agree to state the resulting value of a as follows: 
-------To the nearest minute if four-place Table VI is used.
To the nearest tenth of a minute if five-place Table IX is used.
In finding a logarithm from a table, we state the result to the number of 
decimal places used in the table. No greater refinement is justified.

49. Problems with approximate data
In computation in this book up to the present time, the emphasis 
has been placed on computing with all the accuracy obtainable from our 
tables, under the assumption that the data are exact. Now, we consider 
computation where the given numbers are only approximate values of 
corresponding quantities. Then, we ask the following questions: (a) With 
what refinement should computation be performed when the data are known 
to have only a specified degree of accuracy? (b) To what stage of accuracy 
should the final results be specified? No hard and fast rules should be 
followed in answering (a) and (b) in any problem. Experience and sound 
judgment are involved in making any final decisions. However, the 
following working rules are satisfactory for problems of the types met 
in this text.
1. In data concerning triangles, it is roughly true that three-place, four- 
place, and five-place accuracy in the lengths of sides correspond to 
accuracy to the nearest 10', 1', and .1', respectively, in the angles.
2. In computing, use four-place or five-place tables according as the 
data are accurate to three or to four places.
3. Round off final results to the same number of places as were specified 
in the data.
In stating that certain data have four-place accuracy, for instance, 
we shall mean that the angular data are accurate to the nearest minute, 
and the lengths to four significant digits.
Note 1. Rule 2 is a suggestion to carry one protective place beyond the stage 
to which final results will be rounded off. Naturally, there is no objection to 
carrying two protective places, if convenient.
Hereafter in this book, assume that the data in any applied problem 
stated tn words are not exact but are the result of measurement, with 
accuracy limited to the significant places in the given numbers. Answers 
to exercises will be listed as they appear after being rounded off to a 
number of places which appear reasonably justified by the data. It 
is admitted that, at times, different and equally valid decisions might 
be made about the number of significant places in the answers.

50. Projections
In a given plane, let OL be a given line and let CD be any line seg­
ment. Then, if A and B are the feet of the perpendiculars from C and 
D to OL, we call AB the projection of CD on OL. If OL and CD (pos­
sibly extended) form an acute angle a, as in Figure 37, we construct ECH

Thus, the projection of CD on OL equals CD ■ cos a, and the projection 
of CD on a line perpendicular to OL equals CD • sin a, where a is the 
acute angle formed by OL and CD.
Illustration 1. If a train travels 3000 feet along a straight grade inclined 
2° 37' from the horizontal, the horizontal and vertical projections of the path 
of the train are 3000 cos 2° 37' and 3000 sin 2° 37', respectively.
51. Bearing angle for a direction
In a horizontal plane, let any direction be represented by a directed 
line segment or arrow radiating from some fixed point 0, as in Figure 38. 
Frequently, a direction is called a bearing, 
and it may be described by stating the acute 
angle made by it with the north or south 
direction. This angle will be called the 
acute bearing angle for the given direction.
Illustration 1. In Figure 38, the bear­
ing of OA is 20° west of north, abbreviated 
N 20° W. The bearing of OB is S 40° W. To 
describe the bearing of a line, we first write the 
letter N (or S), then the acute angle between 
the given line and ON (or OS), and finally write 
E or W to show on which side the given direc­
tion falls.
Example 1. Find the bearing and distance of K as seen from 0, if K 
is 30.6 miles north and 78.3 miles east of 0.

52. Azimuth
In the navigation of an airplane or a ship, in astronomy, and in
the artillery service, the direction from a point 
G to a point B in the horizontal plane fre­
quently is described by telling the angle 
through which a line GN pointing north must 
be rotated (clockwise) toward the east in order 
to coincide with GB. This angle is called the 
azimuth of B from G, or the azimuth of GB, 
and is an angle a such that 0° a < 360°. 
Frequently, to find the azimuth of a direction, 
we first find its acute bearing angle.
Illustration 1. In Figure 39, the azimuth 
of GB is 220°. The bearing of GB is <8 40° W.
In any problem which refers to the flight of an airplane, or the sailing 
of a ship, we shall assume that the motion is in a horizontal plane, unless 
otherwise specified. Essentially, then, we shall be dealing with a naviga­
tion problem in plane sailing. Other examples will be of the types met 
in the field of plane surveying, where the surveyor is assumed to work 
in a horizontal plane, except as points may be elevated above or below 
it. Plane sailing and plane surveying involve similar mathematical 
details. Problems in the exercises will be based on the minimum of 
technical language already introduced.

 All paths involved in problems 
will be along one or more straight line segments.

53. Vectors
A directed line segment or stroke, such as OP in Figure 40, is called 
a vector. The measure of the length of OP in terms of some linear unit 
--
is called the magnitude of OP. Thus, the defining characteristics of a 
vector are its direction and magnitude. In 
this book, any vectors which are con- 
sidered simultaneously will be coplanar 
(all in some given plane). We shall as­
sume that a vector can be moved as we 
please in the plane, as long as the vector’s 
length and direction are unaltered.

APPLICATIONS OF RIGHT TRIANGLES
63
Let two vectors OF and OP be given, and let them be drawn with a 
common initial point 0, as in Figure 40. Then, we define the sum, or 
the resultant, of OP and OF as the vector OR which is the diagonal of 
—> 
—>
the parallelogram having OP and OF as adjacent sides. This definition 
is referred to as the parallelogram law for the addition of vectors. We call 
OP and OF the components of OR along the lines of the corresponding 
sides of the parallelogram.
The resultant of OP and OF, in Figure 40, may also be found as 
follows: draw OP; then place the initial point of OF on the terminal point 
of OP, giving PR, which locates R and determines OR.
Illustration 1. Any force or velocity
*
 possesses a direction and a magnitude, 
and hence can be represented geometrically by a properly directed vector, with 
the measure of its length in some linear unit equal to the measure of the force 
or velocity in some physical unit. In Figure 40, let OF and OP represent any 
two forces pulling (or acting) simultaneously on an object at 0. Then, in 
- > '■>
physics it is proved that the combined effect of forces OF and OP is the same 
as the effect of the single resultant force represented by the resultant vector OR. 
This fact is referred to as the parallelogram law for the composition of forces.
—> 
>
If OF and OP represent velocities simultaneously imposed on an object at 0, 
the resultant velocity is represented by OR. Thus, velocities also obey the 
parallelogram law in composition.
The components of a vector OR along two 
perpendicular lines ON and OE arc the vectors 
obtained by projecting OR on ON and OE. In 
Figure 41, the magnitudes of the components are
OB = OR cos 0;
OD = OR sin (3.
The given vector OR is the resultant of its com­
ponents OB and OD. In finding OB and OD in 
Figure 41, we say that we have resolved the given 
vector into components.
Illustration 2. In Figure 41, OR represents a force of 150 pounds acting 
in the direction N 31° E; the acute bearing angle is /3 = 31°.
OB = 150 cos 31° = 129; OD = 150 sin 31° = 77.
The north component is 129 pounds; the east component is 77 pounds.
* And other physical quantities, such as accelerations. In this text, vector appli­
cations of a physical nature will involve only forces and velocities.

APPLICATIONS OF RIGHT TRIANGLES
Any physical quantity possessing a magnitude and a direction can be 
represented as a vector. If a given class of such quantities has the 
further property that composition of simultaneously acting quantities 
obeys the parallelogram law, we refer to the quantities as vector quanti­
ties. Thus, forces and velocities are vector quantities.
Note 1. If the weight of an object is w pounds, the attraction of gravity 
on it is a vertical force of w pounds acting at the object’s center of gravity.
Note 2. The direction in which the nose of an airplane or the prow of a 
ship is pointed in its motion is called the heading. The direction of the motion 
relative to the ground is called the course of the airplane or ship.
Example 1. An airplane is headed east with an airspeed of 240 miles 
per hour. A north wind is blowing with a speed of 40 miles per hour. 
Find the airplane’s groundspeed and course.
—>
Solution. 1. In Figure 42, OH represents the wind velocity, with which

called the airspeed vector, whose magnitude

and OP. We call OG the groundspeed vec-

Comment. The smallest angle between the heading and course of an air­
plane is called the drift angle. It is 9° 28' in Example 2; the wind blows the 
airplane 9° 28' off its heading.

*54. Problems involving forces and velocities
Example 1. Find the force which is just sufficient to keep a 1000- 
pound weight from sliding down a plane inclined 47° to the horizontal, 
under the assumption that there is no friction.
Solution. 1. The attraction of gravity on 
the weight A, in Figure 43, is a vertical force 
AW, of 1000 pounds. We resolve AW into two 
>
perpendicular components, AK acting along the 
-- >
plane and AH perpendicular to the plane. Force 
->
AH creates pressure on the plane and is coun- 
teracted by its supports. Force AK acts to move 
the weight A downward. Hence, we must apply 
force AF equal in magnitude to AK and opposite 
in direction.
2. In &KAW, 6 = 43° and AW = 1000. Thus we obtain
AK = 1000 cos 43° = 731.4.
Hence, a force of 731.4 lb. would hold the weight steady. Any greater force 
would move the weight upward. The favorable difference between 1000 and 
"31.4 shows the advantage of the inclined ramp. A force slightlv greater than 
10001b. is necessary to raise the weight vertically.

are exhibited in Figure 45.

equal to the same function of the reference angle for 9. That is, 
(any function of 9) - ± (same function of reference angle a),

56. Functional notation
If a variable y is a function (not necessarily a trigonometric function) 
of a variable x, we have agreed to call y the dependent variable and x 
the independent variable. Sometimes we represent a function of a 
specified independent variable by a symbol such as /(x), H(x), or K(0). 
The letter in parentheses is the independent variable. The letter at the 
left of the parentheses is merely a convenient name letter or abbrevia­
tion for the function. We read “f(x) ” as “ the f-function of x,” or simply 
“f of x.” If f(x) is a function of x and h is a particular value for x, then
f(h) represents the value of /(x) when x = h. 
(1)
Illustration 1. If/(x) = 3+ + 2r — 7, then
/(2) = 3(22) +2(2) -7 = 9;
/(- 3) = 3(9) + 2( — 3) - 7 = 14.
Illustration 2. If/(x) = sin x°, then/(90) = sin 90° = 1.
Similarly, a function of two independent variables x and y may be 
represented by F(x, y), G(x, y), etc.

57. Review concerning equations and identities
An equation whose sides are equal for all permissible values of any 
variables involved is called an identical equation, or simply an identity. 
An equation whose members are not equal for all permissible values of 
the variables is called a conditional equation.
Illustration 1. The equation (x + y')2 = x2 + 2xy + y2 is an identity, 
because it is true for all values of x and y. The equation x — 2 = 0 is a con­
ditional equation which is true only when x = 2.
Illustration 2. The trigonometric equation sin 9 = 1/csc 9 is an identity 
because it is true for all values of the angle 9 for which esc 9 is defined. The 
equation sin 9 = 1 is a conditional equation because it is true only when 9 = 90°, 
or when 9 is an angle coterminal with 90°.
The word equation by itself may be used in referring to both identities 
and conditional equations. However, the unqualified word equation 
usually applies to a conditional equation.
Illustration 3. The reciprocal relations of page 11 are identities, for all 
values of 9 for which the expressions are defined.
Illustration 4. Certain relations exist between the functions of any 
two angles whose sum or difference is an integral multiple of 90°. The resulting 
identities will be called reduction formulas.

58. Periodicity of the trigonometric functions
If an angle 0 varies from one value to another, the terminal side of 
0 in standard position revolves about the origin of the coordinate system. 
The positions of the terminal side of 0, and hence the values of its trigo­
nometric functions, repeat themselves periodically at intervals of 360c 
in the values of 0. Thus, for every value of 0, the angles 0 and (0 + 360°), 
or (6 — 360°), are coterminal and their functions have identical values. 
Hence, we obtain the following reduction formulas:
[any function of (0 + 360°)J = (same function of 0); 
(1)
[any function of (0 — 360°)J = (same function of 0). 
(2)
On account of (1), we say that each trigonometric function of the variable 
angle 0 is a periodic function of 0 with 360° as a period. Equation (1) 
abbreviates six identities:
sin (0 + 360°)=sin 0; cos (0 + 360°)=cos 0; • ■ esc (0 + 360°)=csc 0.
Illustration 1. From (1) and (2), the values of sin 0 repeat at intervals 
of 360° in the values of 0, if we increase or decrease 9. Thus, sin 9 repeats the 
value sin 30° = | at intervals of 360°:
| = sin 30° = sin (30° + 360°) = sin (30° + 720°) = etc.;
= sin (30° - 360°) = sin (30° - 720°) = etc.
Identities (1) and (2) are equivalent, although in different notation. 
Thus, either (1) or (2) states that, if two angles differ by 360°, correspond­
ing functions of the two angles are equal.
★Note 1. If fix) is a function of x, and p is a constant such that/(z+p) =/(x) 
for all values of x, we say that /(z) is periodic and has p as a period. Then, also,
fix + 2p) = /[(a: + p) + p] = ffx + p) = fix),
so that 2p is seen to be a period. Similarly, 3p, 4p, or, in general, np is a period 
where n is any positive integer.

63. General reduction formulas
We have proved special cases of the following identities, in which 
“multiple of 90°” means an “integral multiple, n ■ 90°,” where n is an 
integer.
I Any function of an 1 
, ,

In (1) or (2), for any given function of 0 and any given multiple of 90°, 
just one sign, + or —, applies on the right-hand side regardless of the 
value of 0. Any particular case of (1) or (2) can be proved geometrically, 
as in Sections 60 and 61, or may be established by use of other reduction 
formulas previously proved.
*
 We shall use (1) and (2) without further 
proof.
Note 1. The student should memorize (1) and (2) and the most useful 
special cases, such as: the periodicity relations for (360° + O'); the periodicity 
relations tan (180° + 0) = tan 0 and cot (180° + fl) = cot fl; the expressions 
for sin (—fl), cos (—fl), and tan (—fl) from page 71; the cofunction relations 
for (90° - fl).
Example 1. Express cot (0 + 270°) in terms of a function of 0.
Solution. 1. From (2), since 270° = 3(90°), and since 3 is odd,
cot (fl + 270°) = ± tan fl. 
(3)
2. To determine the sign + or — in (3), think of the case where fl is acute. 
Then, (fl + 270°) is in quadrant IV where the cotangent is negative. Since 
tan fl is positive if fl is acute, we use the minus sign in (3) to give a negative right­
hand side. Hence, cot (fl + 270°) = — tan fl. The special case, with fl acute, 
was convenient in determining the sign.
Summary. To obtain any particular reduction formula, use (1) or (2) to 
write the formula with an ambiguous sign. Then, determine the proper 
sign by checking signs on the two sides for the case where 0 is acute.
If desired, reduction formulas can be used to accomplish the aims of 
Section 55 where reference angles were employed. That is, if y is a 
given angle in any quadrant, we may express any function of y in terms 
of some function of a corresponding acute angle 0 as follows:
1. Express y as a sum or difference of the nearest integral multiple 
of 90° and an acute angle 0 (which may be taken at most equal to 45°).
2. Use (1) or (2) to write a formula for the desired function of y in 
terms of a function of 0, with “ + ” or “ — ” used on the right-hand side 
according as the function of y is positive or negative.

66. Variation of cot 6, sec 0, esc 9
Since cot 0 = 1/tan 0, the angle 0 has no cotangent when tan 0 = 0, 
because 1/0 has no meaning. However, if 0 varies so that tan 0 approaches 
the limit zero, which occurs if 0 approaches 0°, 180°, etc., the absolute 
value of 1/tan 0 grows large without bound, or cot 0 becomes infinite. 
Thus, we obtain cot 0 = oo (cotangent 0 is infinite) if 0 is any angle whose 
terminal side in standard position falls on OX. That is,
cot 0° = oo, cot 180° = oo, cot 360 = oo °, etc.
Illustration 1. We recall sec 6 = l/cos0 and esc 0 = 1/sin 0. If 0 is 
any angle whose terminal side in standard position falls on O Y, then cos 0 = 0 
and sec 0 = oo ; thus, sec 90° = co , sec 270° = co, etc. If 0 is any angle whose 
terminal side falls on OX, then sin 0 = 0 and we obtain esc 0 = oo; that is, 
we have
esc 0° = co, esc 180° = oo, esc 360° = oo, etc.

angle 0 are best remembered in connection with later graphs.
67. General terminology of graphing
A solution of an equation in two variables x and y is a pair of values 
of x and y which satisfy the equation. We may interpret any pair of 
corresponding values of x and y as the coordinates of a point in an x, y 
coordinate plane. Then, we define the graph of an equation in x and y 
as the set of all points whose coordinates (x, y) form solutions of the eguev- 
tion. To graph an equation, we compute representative solutions, and 
then draw a smooth curve (or curves) through the corresponding points 
(fi, y)-
Illustration 1. To graph 3x — by = 15, we compute the following solu­
tions. We substitute x = 0 and obtain y = — 3; thus, (x = 0, y = — 3) 
is a solution. If y = O th

Let f(x) represent any function (not necessarily a trigonometric 
function) of a variable x, and let y = f(x). Then, we define the graph 
of the function f(x) as the graph of the corresponding equation y = /(x).

graph recedes from the a;-axis beyond all bounds, because the absolute value 
of y grows large without bound. We can approach as closely as we desire to 
any asymptote, but never reach it, by receding sufficiently far from the z-axis 
on the graph. To construct Figure 56, the asymptotes were drawn first as 
guide lines; then, the curves were drawn through points obtained from the

With any periodic function of a variable x, the smallest period for I 
the function is usually referred to simply as THE period. Thus, THE j 
period of tan x is 180°; THE period of the functions sin x, cos x, sec x, 1 
esc x is 360°.

----- Example 1.—Describe the variation of sec x on the range 0° to 360°.
Solution. From Figure 58, if x increases continuously from 0° to 90°, the 
graph rises, or sec x increases from 1 to plus infinity (+ oo); as x increases from 
90° to 180°, the graph rises, or sec x increases from — oo to — 1; as x increases 
from 180° to 270°, sec x decreases from — 1 to — oo ; as x increases from 270° 
to 360°, sec x decreases from + oo to 1.
Illustration 3. The only solution of the equation tan x = 1 on the 
range 0° to 90° is x = 45°. Therefore, since tan x has 180° as a period, the 
solutions of tan x = 1 on the range 0° to 360° are x — 45° and x = 225°, where 
we were aided by Figure 56 in our decision.
Illustration 4. From Figure 58, sec x = co if x = 90°, 270°. Since 
sec x is a periodic function with 360° as a period, other solutions of sec x — oo 
are x = 90° — 360° and x = 270° — 360°. Hence, the solutions of sec x = oo 
on the range - 360° to 360° are x = 270°, 90°, - 90°, - 270°.

FUNDAMENTAL CHARACTERISTICS OF THE FUNCTIONS
83
Illustration 5. To find all values of arccsc (— 1) on the range — 360° 
to 360°, we first let x = arccsc (—1). Then, esc x = — 1 and hence x = 270°. 
By periodicity, we also find x = 270° — 360° = — 90°. Thus, on the range 
— 360° to 360°, arccsc (—1) has the values — 90° and 270°.
Note 1. A function of x (not necessarily a trigonometric function) is said 
to be continuous at a value of x where the graph of the function is continuous 
in the geometrical sense, and is said to be discontinuous at a value of x where 
there is a break in the graph. Thus, sin x and cos x are continuous at all values 
of x. Tan x, sec x, cot x, esc x are continuous except where the functions are 
undefined.

RADIAN MEASURE
70. Radian measure
Previously in this book, angles have been measured in degrees and 
their fractional parts. Hereafter, we shall frequently use an angular 
unit called a radian
*
 which is of fundamental importance in advanced 
mathematics and in its applications.
Definition I. One radian is the measure of an angle which, if its 
vertex is placed at the center of a circle, subtends on the circumference 
an arc equal to the radius of the circle.
Illustbation 1. In Figure 60, the measure 
of Z BOO is one radian. Hence, the length of 
the subtended arc BC is r.
Since an arc of length r subtends an 
angle of one radian at the center of a circle, 
the whole circumference, with length 2rrr, 
subtends an angle of 2?r X (one radian) or 
2tt radians, f In degrees, the whole circum­
ference subtends 360°. Hence,

Thus, from (3), to change degree measure to radian measure, multiply 
the number of degrees, in decimal form, by tf/180. To change from 
radians to degrees, multiply the number of radians by 180/tt. Since 
180° = tt radians, any angle which is a convenient multiple of 180° 
is the same multiple of tt radians.

73. Trigonometric graphs, with angles measured in radians
Suppose that y is a trigonometric function of x, which is the measure 
of a variable angle in terms of a certain angular unit. Then, in drawing 
the graph of the function, if there is no specified relation between the 
scales on the coordinate axes, no standard shape for the graph is associated 
with the angular unit.
In most of the applications of trigonometric graphs in calculus and 
in many parts of applied mathematics, the graphs are made subject to 
the following agreements, which we adopt in the remainder of this 
chapter. The graphs obtained will be called standard graphs.
I. If an expression is given for the value of an angle, this value is the 
measure of the angle in radians.
II. If y is a trigonometric function
*
 of a variable x, in graphing the 
function we shall use the same unit of distance for the number scales 
on the x-axis and y-axis of the coordinate system.
Example 1. Graph y = sin x on the range x = 0 to x = tt, inclusive.
Solution. 1. Let y be the degree measure corresponding to x radians. 
We select values of y and find sin x from knowledge of sin y. We list values 
of x first as multiples of tt and then as decimals, from Table XIII.
2. To form Figure 63, select a unit for distance and lay off a scale with this 
unit on both OX and OY. Then, plot (x, y) from the table as usual.

algebraic and trigonometric functions are met.

*74. Graphs of more general trigonometric functions
Previously, in any reference to a trigonometric function of x, we have 
meant one of the fundamental functions sin x, cos x, tan x, cot x, sec x, 
esc x. More generally, if the measure of an angle is expressed in terms 
of a variable x, then any fundamental function of this angle, or any 
algebraic combination of such functions, is called a trigonometric function 
of x.
2. In drawing a graph which does not have to be extremely accurate, 
we proceed as follows in order to avoid recalling many decimal values for x. 
First, choose a unit for distance in Figure 64 and mark the corresponding 
scales on OX and OY, in units. Locate it and 2ir accurately on OX as 3.14 
and 6.28. By use of compasses or dividers, or by visual estimation, without 
using decimal values, mark on OX the points Jtt, |tt, |tt, and corresponding 
points between x = jtt and x = 2ir. Then, plot the points (x, y) from the 
table, and add points between x = and x = 2ir from knowledge of the 
symmetry of the wave for y = cos x.

Example 1. Graph the function y = sin 3x from x = 0 to x = 2ir.
Solution. 1. If x increases from 0 to 2tt, then 3x increases from 0 to 6ir, 
and sin 3z passes through the values obtained from sin 9 if 6 varies from 0 
to 6tt. Thus, on the range x = 0 to x = 2tt, the graph of y = sin 3x will con­
sist of three waves, whereas the graph of y = sin x consists of just one wave. The 
waves for sin x and sin 3x have the same amplitude.
2. Since zero values of sin 9 occur at intervals of tt in the values of 6, zero 
values of sin 3z occur at intervals of tt in the values of 3x, or ir/3 in the values 
of x. Hence, integral multiples of tt/6 are used for x to obtain the crests, troughs, 
and nodes of the waves, giving the full line graph in Figure 65.
Y
y = sin 3x:---- . j/ = |sin3»:------ .

Comment. Since sin 6 has the period 2ir,
sin 3r = sin (3x + 2tt) = sin 3(x + fir).
Or, sin 3x is a periodic function with the period 2ir/3. This corresponds to the 
fact that there are three complete waves in Figure 65. Similarly, if n is a posi­
tive number,* sin nx, cos nx, sec nx, esc nx are periodic functions of x with the 
period 2ir/n. The functions tan nx and cot nx are periodic functions of x with 
the period ir/n because tan 0 and cot 0 have the period it.

Figure 65, where each wave has one half the amplitude of the wave for 
y = sin 3z. Values of y for graphing y — | sin 3x would be one half times those 
for y = sin 3z in Example 1.
75. Composition of ordinates
Let a: be a variable and suppose that a function F(x) is expressed 
as the sum of two functions, F(x) = g(x) + h(x). In certain cases it may 
be desirable to obtain the graph of F(x) by first graphing g(x) and h(x) 
on one coordinate system, and then adding, geometrically, corresponding 
ordinates of the graphs of g(x) and h(x). If this is done, we say that 
is graphed by composition (or addition) of ordinates.
* Usually an integer in applications in this text.

92
RADIAN MEASURE
Example 1. Graph y = sin x + cos x by composition of ordinates.
Solution. 1. The graphs of yi = sin x and y2 = cos x are shown in 
Figure 66.
2. To graph y = sin x + cos x, we note that y = yv + y2. Hence, for 
any value of x, we obtain the ordinate y by geometrically adding y-L and y2, 
by use of dividers, with attention paid to the algebraic signs of y2 and y2.
3. If yi = 0, then y = ; this gives points A, B, C on the desired graph; 
D and E are obtained where y2 = 0. If t/i = — y2, then y = 0; this gives F 
and G. Various other ordinates were added. The graph is the full-line curve 
in Figure 66.

77. Proofs of identities
In applications of trigonometry, particularly in calculus, it is some­
times necessary to prove that one trigonometric expression is equal to 
another for all values of the angles involved. Or, it may be necessary 
to prove that one trigonometric expression can be transformed into some 
other expression. Thus, applications of trigonometry frequently make 
it necessary to prove trigonometric identities. The fundamental identities 
can sometimes be used to establish other identities. On account of the 
nature of the applications of identities, the following method should

96
FUNDAMENTAL IDENTITIES
be emphasized, although other logically satisfactory procedures will be 
illustrated.
I. To prove an identity, leave one side unaltered, and change the 
other side until it has the same form as the first side.
Example 1. Prove the identity:
tan 0 + 2 cot 6 = sin1 2 0 + 2 cos2 0

involves only sin 0 and cos 0, we express the left-hand side in terms of sin 0 and 
cos 0, by use of page 93:

The following suggestions are useful in proving identities.
a. If possible, avoid using formulas involving radicals.
b. Perhaps express all functions in terms of sines and cosines.
c. If one side of the identity involves just one function, perhaps express 
everything on the other side in terms of this function.

78. Trigonometric equations in one unknown
The simplest type of trigonometric equation is one which is linear 
in one function of one unknown angle 0, and thus gives the value of this 
function immediately. Then, there are usually two corresponding values 
for 0 on the range 0° 
0 < 360°, as we observed geometrically in prob­
lems in Section 16, page 16. On account of the periodicity of the trigo­
nometric functions, any trigonometric equation usually has infinitely 
many solutions, with unbounded absolute values. In this book, unless 
otherwise directed, to solve a trigonometric equation in 0 will mean to 
obtain only those solutions which are on the range 0° g 0 < 360°.

FUNDAMENTAL IDENTITIES
99
Summary. To find 0 when one function of 0 is given:
I. From a trigonometric table, or from memory  find an angle a between 
0° and 90°, inclusive, whose corresponding function is the absolute value 
of the given function of 0.
*
II. From knowledge of the signs of the functions in the various quadrants, 
locate the values of 0 on the range 0° 
0 < 360° having a as the refer­
ence angle.
Example 1. Solve: 2 cos 0—1 = 0.
Solution. 1. This equation is linear (of the first degree) in cos 0. We 
find 2 cos 0 = 1 or cos 0 = |.
2. Recall that cos 60° = % and that the cosine is positive in quadrants I 
and IV. Hence, in quadrant IV, 0 has that value for which the reference angle 
is 60°. The solutions are 0 = 60° and 0 = 300°.
Comment. Other solutions are — 60°, 420°, 660°, etc., which are obtained 
by adding integral multiples of 360° to 60° or 300°. For every integral value 
of n, each of the following expressions is a solution, or these expressions give 
the complete set of solutions of the equation:
0 = 60° + n(360°); 
0 = 300° + n(360°).
Example 2. Solve: cos 0 = — .9872.
Solution. 1. Let a be the acute angle such that cos a = .9872. From 
Table VII, a = 9° 10'.
2. The cosine is negative in quadrants II and III. The values for 0 are 
the angles in these quadrants having 9° 10' as the reference angle. Hence, 
9 = 180° ± 9° 10', or the solutions are 0 = 189° 10', 170° 50'.
A trigonometric equation in one angle 0, which is in the quadratic form 
in one function of 0± can be solved by use of methods met in dealing with 
quadratic equations in algebra. In particular, solution by factoring 
may apply.
Example 3. Solve: 2 sin2 x — sin x — 1 = 0.
Solution. 1. We say that this equation is in the quadratic form in sin x 
because, if we should let v = sin x (which is unnecessary) the equation would 
become 2v2 — v — 1 = 0. To solve the given equation we first factor:
(2 sin x + l)(sin x — 1) = 0. 
(1)
2. The equation is satisfied if:
2 sin x + 1 =0; 
then sin x = — | and x = 210° and x = 330°;
sin x — 1 — 0; 
then sin x = 1 and x = 90°.
To solve an equation by use of factoring, the equation must first be 
written with one member zero.
* Functions of 0°, 30°, 45°, 60°, 90° should be thoroughly reviewed at this point.

100
FUNDAMENTAL IDENTITIES
Example 4. 
Solve: sin x cos x = cos x.
Solution. 1. Subtract cos x from both sides; then factor:
sin x cos x — cos x = 0; cos x(sin x — 1) = 0.
2. If cos x = 0, then x = 90° or 270°. If sin x — 1 = 0, then x = 90°.
Incorrect Solution. Divide both sides by cos x: sin x = 1; x = 90°.
Comment. If both sides of an equation are divided by an expression in­
volving the unknowns, solutions may be lost. Thus, the division by cos x 
caused the loss of the solution 270° for which cos x = 0.
Example 5. Find all values of arctan <3 on the range 0° to 360°.
Solution. Let x = arctan <3. Then, tan x = <3. The solutions are 
x = 60° and x = 240°. That is, arctan <3 = 60° and arctan <3 = 240°.
Illustration 1. To solve sin2 x = %, we extract square roots, obtaining 
sin x = ± <j = ± <2/2. If sin x = <2/2, then x = 45° or x = 135°. 
If sin x = — <2/2, then x = 225° or x = 315°. Thus, we have four solutions.

79. Equations solved by the aid of identities
In solving a trigonometric equation, our aim is to find one or more 
equations, each involving just one function of one unknown angle, which 
are equivalent to the given equation. To accomplish this aim, it may 
be useful to modify given expressions by use of known identities. The 
following actions should be thought of as possibilities.
I. Express each function of x in terms of one function.
II. Express each function of x in terms of sin x and cos x.
Example 1. Solve: sin 0 — cos 0 = 0.
Solution. 1. Divide by cos 3; 
—/ — 1=0, or tan 0 — 1=0.
cos 0
2. Hence, tan 0=1; the solutions are 0 = 45° and 0 = 225°.
Comment. If cos 0 = 0, then 0 = 90° or 0 = 270°. By substitution, we 
see that neither value is a solution of the given equation. Hence, no solutions 
were lost on dividing both sides by cos 0.
Example 2. Solve: tan2 x + 3 sec x + 3 = 0.
Solution. 1. Use tan2 x = sec2 x — 1: 
sec2 x + 3 sec x + 2 = 0.
2. 
Factor: 
(sec x + 2)(sec x + 1) = 0.
3. If sec x + 2 = 0, then sec x = — 2; x = 120° and x = 240°.
4. If sec x + 1 = 0, then sec x = — 1; x = 180°. The solutions are
120°, 180°, 240°.
80. Extraneous solutions
In solving an equation, it is sometimes useful to employ one or both 
of the following operations.
I. 
Square both sides, or raise both to any specified power.
II. Multiply both sides by an expression involving the unknowns.
Example 1. Solve: cos x + 1 = sin x. 
(1)
Solution. 1. Use sin x = ± Vl — cos2x:
cos x + 1 = ± Vl — cos2 x.
2. Square both sides: 
(1 + cos x)2 = 1 — cos2 x;
1+2 cos x + cos2 x = 1 — cos2 x; 2 cos x + 2 cos2 x = 0;
2 cos z(l + cos x') = 0. 
(2)
From (2), x = 90°, x = 180°, or x = 270°; these may be solutions.
3. Test all values of x by substituting in (1). Test x = 270°.
Does cos 270° + 1 = sin 270°? Does 0 + 1 = - 1? No.
Hence, 270° is not a solution. Similarly, we find that 90° and 180° are solutions.

102
FUNDAMENTAL IDENTITIES
The new equation obtained by use of (I) or (II) may have solutions 
not possessed by the given equation. Such solutions are called extraneous | 
solutions. Hence, if (I) or (II) is used, all values obtained for the un- j 
knowns must be tested in the original equation to reject extraneous solu­
tions, if any. This procedure was used in Example 1.

Note 1. If a and (3 are acute and a + |3 > 90°, the preceding proof applies 
without a single change in wording if the same construction and letters are 
used in the corresponding Figure 68. In this proof, it would be understood 
that the value of any line segment parallel to a coordinate axis is positive or 
negative according as the segment’s direction 
is the same as or opposite to the positive di­
rection on the axis. An analytical proof 
that (I) and (II) hold for all angles a and /3 
is given in Note 10 of the Appendix, on the 
basis of the geometrical proof for the case 
where a and /S are acute.
Formula (I) states that the sine of 
the sum of two angles equals the sine of 
one of the angles times the cosine of the 
other angle plus the cosine of the first times 
the sine of the second. We call (I) and 
(II) the addition formulas for the sine 
and cosine. Without further discus­
sion, we shall proceed under the assump­
tion that (I) and (II) are true for all 
values of a and /3.

Product formulas
We recall the following identities:
sin (a + 
/3) = sin a cos (3 + cos a sin (3; 
(1)

/3) = sin a cos /3 — cos a sin /3; 
(2)

/3) = cos a cos /3 — sin a sin /?; 
(3)
cos (a — 
/3) = cos a cos /3 + sin a sin /3. 
(4)
On adding corresponding sides in (1) and (2), we obtain the following 
formula (XIV). On adding (3) and (4) we obtain (XV). On subtracting 
(3) from (4) we obtain (XVI). On subtracting (2) from (1) we obtain 
(XVII).
2 sin a cos (3 = sin (a + /3) + sin (a — /3). 
(XIV)
2 cos a cos /3 = cos (a + /3) + cos (a — /3). 
(XV)
2 sin a sin /3 = cos (a — /3) — cos (a + (3). 
(XVI)
2 cos a sin /3 = sin (a + /3) — sin (a — /3). 
(XVII)

their main importance in this text is that they give a means for proving formulas 
in the next section.

88. 
Miscellaneous identities
*
In proving identities, the following suggestions may be useful.
I. If possible, avoid introducing radicals.
II. 
Usually, it is best to express any cotangent, secant, or cosecant of a 
complicated angle in terms of the tangent, cosine, or sine, because of the 
nature of the formulas we have derived.
III. It may be convenient to express all functions in terms of functions 
of a single angle.
Example 1. Prove the identity:
cos 3z = cos x — 4 sin2 x cos x.
Solution. By use of (II), 
cos 3x = cos (z + 2x) = cos x cos 2x — sin x sin 2x
= cos x (1 — 2 sin2 z) — sin z(2 sin x cos z)
= cos z — 4 sin2 z cos z. Q.E.D.
* In a brief course, the remainder of this chapter could be omitted without 
eliminating later content.

89. Miscellaneous equations
An equation in x which explicitly states the value of one function of 
some multiple of x should be solved without alteration, by inspection or 
perhaps with the aid of a trigonometric table.
ExampleI. Solve: 
sin 3x =
Solution. 1. Recall that sin 30° = | = sin 150°. Either of these angles, 
plus any integral multiple of 360°, is a value of “3x” which satisfies the equa­
tion. Hence, as values for 3x, we obtain 30°, 390°, 750°, 150°, 510°, 870°, etc.
2. If 3x = 30°, then z = 10°; if 3x = 390°, then x = 130°; etc. The solu­
tions for x between 0° and 360° are 10°, 50°, 130°, 170°, 250°, 290°.
Comment. We needed all values of 3x between 0° and 3(360°) so that, after : 
dividing by 3, we would have all values of x between 0° and 360°.
To solve a trigonometric equation, we aim to find one or more equa­
tions, each involving only one function of one angle, whose solutions 
include all solutions of the given equation.
Example 2.
Solution. 1.

1 — cos x = 0, or (2 cos x + l)(cos x — 1) = 0.
2. We solve 2 cos x + 1 = 0 and cos x — 1 = 0 and obtain
x = 0°, x = 120°, x = 240°.

NINE
OBLIQUE TRIANGLES
90. 
Introduction
In any triangle ABC, as in Figure 70, let a, ft, y be the angles at A, B, 
C, and let a, b, c be the lengths of the 
corresponding opposite sides. The angles 
C
a, ft, y and sides a, b, c are called the parts 
a
of the triangle. We can construct a tri- 
/ y -----
angle if we are given any three of its 
parts, of which at least one is a side. Ac 
B
Such data fall into four classes. 
Fig. 70
I. 
Given two angles and a side.
II. 
Given two sides and an angle opposite one of them.
III. 
Given two sides and the included angle.
IV. 
Given three sides.
In this chapter, we shall develop methods for computing the unknown 
parts of a triangle under each of Cases I to IV. The computation of 
the unknown parts is called the solution of the triangle.
91. 
Law of cosines
In any triangle, the square of any side is equal to the sum of the squares 
of the other sides minus twice their product times the cosine of their in­
cluded angle. That is,

92. 
Solution of triangles by the law of cosines
The law of cosines is not well fitted for logarithmic computation. 
However, the formulas are used frequently without logarithms, particu­
larly in physics. If a table of squares is available, or if convenient num­
bers are involved, problems of certain types can be solved efficiently by 
use of the law of cosines and natural values of the trigonometric functions.
Outline. Application of the law of cosines to solve £\ABC under 
Case IV, that is, to find the angles when all sides are given.

2. Check the solution by means of a + ft + 7 = 180°.
