95. Solution of Case II, the ambiguous case
If two sides of a triangle ABC and an angle opposite one of these 
sides are given, we shall find that there may exist two solutions, or just 
one solution, or no solution for the triangle, depending on the given values. 
Since there may be two solutions, Case II is called the ambiguous case.
Note 1. Recall that, if k is a positive number less than 1, the equation 
sin a = k has two positive solutions less than 180°, one acute and the other ob­
tuse. This existence of two solutions is the fact which accounts for the possi­
bility of two triangles as solutions in Case II.
If the given parts of a triangle ABC are a, b, a, any triangle satisfying 
the data may be obtained geometrically as follows.
1. Construct a with, one side AD horizontal. On the other side, measure 
distance b from A to locate vertex C.
2. With C as center and radius equal to side a, strike an arc. The 
vertex B may then be located at any point where this arc cuts AD.

OBLIQUE TRIANGLES
127
In Figures 75 and 80, the arc does not meet AD, and hence there 
is no solution. In Figure 76, the arc is tangent to AD and there is just 
one position for B, with /3 = 90°. In Figure 77, the arc cuts AD twice, 
at B and Bi; hence there are two solutions, triangles AB fit and ABC. 
In Figure 78 and in Figure 79, there is just one solution.
In a triangle ABC, let h be the perpendicular distance from C to side 
AB. Then, as in Figure 76, sin a = h/b orh = b sin a. In Figures 75-77 
where a < b and a < 90°, we observe that we have no solution, just 
one solution, or two solutions according as side a, the radius of the arc, 
is less than, equal to, or greater than h.
Note 2. The preceding remarks and Figures 75-80 justify the following 
summary of possibilities in case the given parts under the ambiguous Case II 
are a,b, a:
{
a < b sin a; no solution (Figure 75).
a = b sin a; just one solution, with fi = 90° (Figure 76).
a > b sin a, but a < b\ two solutions (Figure 77).
a ft; just one solution (Figure 78).
> 90° / fl > one s°lull°n (Figure 79).
= 
( a b; no solution (Figure 80).
Instead of memorizing the preceding summary, the student is advised to draw 
a figure roughly to scale for each problem. Then, usually, the number of solu­
tions to be expected can be determined easily without formulas.
Outline. To solve A ABC under Case II, with the given parts a, b, a:
1. 
Construct the triangle approximately to scale.
2. Find fi by use of sin = b sin a/a. If this gives
sin /3 > 1, or log sin /3 > 0, there is no solution;
sin /3 = 1, or log sin = 0, then fi = 90°, just one solution;
sin /3 < 1, find one acute and one obtuse value for fi.
3. Discard any value of fi for which a + /3 = 180°; for each value of 
fi, compute y = 180° — (a + fi).
4. 
For each pair of values of fi and y, compute c = a sin 7/sin a.
Note 3. A solution under Case II may be checked as in Case I.

In a triangle, the difference of any two sides, divided by their sum, IM
equals the tangent of one half of the difference of the opposite angles 
divided by the tangent of one half of their sum:

These formulas are called Mollweide’s equations, in recognition of their use

triangle. The proofs of (1) and (2) are similar to the derivation of the law 
of tangents (see Note 11 of the Appendix).

99. Tangents of the half-angles
We recall that the bisectors of the angles of a triangle ABC, as in 
Figure 82 on page 134, meet at the center O of the inscribed circle. Let r 
be its radius. Then, in Figure 82,
OD = OE = OF = r.
Later, we shall obtain a formula for r in terms of sides a, b, c. Let s be 
one half of the perimeter of triangle ABC:
s = |(a + b + c).

101. Solution of Case IV by the half-angle formulas
Suppose that all sides of triangle ABC are given. Then, if just one 
of its angles is desired, we may use the preceding formulas for the sine 
or cosine of half of the angle, because then it is unnecessary to find r or its 
logarithm. If all angles of the triangle are desired, it is best to use 
tangents of the half-angles, where we proceed as follows.

136 
OBLIQUE TRIANGLES
Summary. To obtain the angles when the sides are known.
1. 
Compute s, (s — a), (s — h), (s — c), and then log r, by use of
r = y/(s ~ 
~ b)(s ~ c)
2. Find a, (3, y by use °f the tangents of the half-angles.
3. 
Check by use of a + + y = 180°.
In checking, it will not be unusual for the sum of the angles to differ 
from 180° by several units in the last angular place employed. This is 
due to the fact that each angle is obtained by multiplying one half of 
the angle by 2, which doubles any previous error.

103. Summary concerning solution of triangles
I. Given two angles 
and a side.
Solve by law of sines and check by law of tangents.
II. Given two sides 
and an opposite angle.
Solve by law of sines, with particular attention to 
the number of solutions. Check by law of tangents
*
III. Given two sides 
and the included angle.
1. Find the angles by law of tangents and the third 
side by law of sines. Check by law of sines or law 
of tangents
*
2. If only the third side is desired, perhaps use law 
of cosines (not adapted to logarithms').
IV. Given three sides.
1. Solve by half-angle formulas and check by 
a + 0 + 7 = 180°.
2. Solve by law of cosines (not adapted to loga­
rithms) and check by a + @ + y = 180°.
Note 1. Recall the suggestions on page 30 for outlining the solution of a 
problem with data described verbally.

INVERSE TRIGONOMETRIC FUNCTIONS
104. The inverse trigonometric functions
*
Let x and y be variables related by the equation x = sin y. Then, 
y can be described as “an angle whose sine is x,” and we write y = arcsin x, 
which is read “arcsine x.” These remarks are summarized as follows.
Definition I. The expression “arcsin x” represents an angle whose 
sine is x, or the following equations are equivalent:
x = sin y and y = arcsin x. 
(1)
Illustration 1. To find the values of arcsin |, let y = arcsin j. Then 
y satisfies sin y = which has the solutions y = 30° and y = 150°. Other 
solutions are 30° ± 360°, 30° ± 720°, 150° ± 360°, etc. Or, arcsin has in­
finitely many values given by
arcsin | = 30° + n ■ 360° and arcsin | = 150° + n ■ 360°, 
(2)
where n is any integer, positive, negative, or zero.-----------------------------------------
In arcsin x, the variable x is the sine of an angle. Hence, arcsin x~ 
is not defined if | x | > 1, and the range for x is — 1 x 1. If | x | 
1, 1
there are infinitely many values of arcsin x. The equation x = sin y ex- - 
plicitly describes x as a function of y, and implicitly defines y as a function < 
of x. The equivalent equation y = arcsin x serves the inverse purpose i 
of describing y explicitly as a function of x. Hence, we call sin y and ' 
arcsin x inverse functions, and refer to either one as the inverse of the other. 
We notice that x = sin y defines x as a single-valued function of y, whereas 
the function y = arcsin x is an infinitely many-valued function of x. ;
Note 1. In place of “arcsin x,” a common notation for it is “sin-1 x." i 
Either arcsin x or sin-1 x may be read “inverse sine of x,” or “arcsine x,” or 1 
“an angle whose sine is x.” 
i
Similarly, we introduce arccos x, arctan x, arccot x, arcsec x, and | 
arccsc x by paraphrasing Definition I for the various functions. Each 
inverse trigonometric function of x is an infinitely many-valued function ] 
of x for each value of x on its allowable range. 
I
* Previously introduced on page 25. A completely fresh start is being made now. j 
144 
1

INVERSE TRIGONOMETRIC FUNCTIONS 
145
Illustration 2. We read “arctan x” as “ arctangent x.” If y = arctan x 
then x — tan y. The variable x in arctan x represents the tangent of an angle, 
and hence may have any real value; or, the range for z is — oo < x < oo.
Illustration 3. If y = arcsec x, then x = sec y. Hence, we cannot 
have | x | < 1, and the range for x is all values such that | x | 2s 1.
Example 1. Find an expression for all values of arccos (— |).
Solution. Let y — arccos (— |). Then, cos y = — |. Hence, the solu­
tions for y between 0° and 360° are y = 120° and y = 240°. Any value of 
arccos (— J) is coterminal with either 120° or 240°, or all values are given by 
the following expressions, where n may be any integer:
arccos (- J) = 120° + n(360°) and arccos (- |) = 240° + w(360°). 
(3)
Comment 1. Instead of (3), we could say that arccos ( — |) is coterminal 
with either 120° or — 120°, so that arccos (— |) = n(360°) ± 120°.
Comment 2. We may state the results in radian measure. Thus,

Thus, arccos (- .2357) = 103° 38' and arccos (- .2357) = 256° 22'.

function of the other. These two functions are called inverse functions, and 
either is called the inverse of the other. This terminology includes inverse 
trigonometric functions as special cases.

105. Principal values 
-,{
We recall that, if y = arcsin x, infinitely many values of y correspon< 
to any assigned value of x. Sometimes it proves essential to fastel 
special attention on a particular one of these values of y, which is callefl 
the principal value of arcsin x. Similar remarks apply to the other inversfl 
functions. Our definitions of principal values will follow the usage mod 
prevalent in integral calculus, where important applications of principal 
values are met. 
1
L

INVERSE TRIGONOMETRIC FUNCTIONS
147
Definition I. If x § 0, the principal value of any inverse trigonometric 
function of x is specified as that value which lies on the range 0 radians to 
tt/2 radians, inclusive.
Illustration 1. The principal value of arcsin | is |tt (or 30°).
Definition IL The principal value of arctan x*  or arcsin x is that 
value which lies on the range — tf/2 radians to tt/2 radians, inclusive.
Definition III. The principal value of arccos x is that value which 
lies on the range 0 radians to tt radians, inclusive.
Example 1. Find the principal value of arcsin (— |).
Solution. Since sin 30° = J, values of arcsin (—J) are 210°, 330°, — 30°, 
— 150°, etc. The principal value of arcsin (— J) is — tt/6 radians, or — 30°.
In writing any inverse function, we shall commence its name with a 
lower case letter in case an arbitrary value of the function is involved, and 
with a capital letter to indicate the principal value.]
Illustration 2. In Example 1, Arcsin (— J) = — |-tt.
★Note 1. We omit introducing principal value agreements for arccot x, 
arcsec x, arccsc x except as they are covered by Definition I. In texts on 
calculus, it is becoming common to avoid using these inverse functions.
★Note 2. The single-valuedness of Arcsin x, Arccos x, and Arctan x, in con­
trast to the fact that the complete inverse functions are infinitely many-valued, 
is a major reason for the importance of principal values.

107. Introduction to imaginary numbers 
|
By definition, R is a square root of A if R2 = A. Hence, — 1 has no ] 
square root R which is zero, positive, or negative because, in such cases, I 
R2 ig 0 and cannot satisfy R2 = — 1. Accordingly, we extend the number ] 
system by introducing the symbol V— 1 as a new variety of number, 1 
called an imaginary number, with the property that V— 1 ■ V— 1 = — 1. I 
And, for contrast, we then call positive and negative numbers and zero j 
real numbers. For convenience, we let i = V— 1. Then, by definition, | 
i2 = — 1. We agree that the operations of addition, subtraction, multi- | 
plication, and division may be applied to combinations of i and real | 
numbers as if i were an ordinary real literal number, with i2 = — 1.*  i 
Thus, in particular, (— i)2 = i2 = — 1, so that — i, as well as + i, is a | 
square root of — 1.

If a and b are real numbers, we call (a + bi) a complex number, 
whose real part is a and imaginary part is bi. If b + 0, we call (a + bi) 
an imaginary number. A pure imaginary number is one whose real 
part is zero; that is, (a + bi) is a pure imaginary if a = 0 and b 0. 
Any real number a is thought of as a complex number in which the co­
efficient of the imaginary part is zero; that is, a = a + Of. In particular, 
0 means (0 + Of).

can be thought of as (6 + Of).

real numbers, except that hereafter i will always represent V — 1.

± 1, by use of i2 = — 1 and the laws of exponents. We notice that

108. Conjugate complex numbers
The numbers (a + bi) and (a — bi) are called conjugate complex num­
bers, and either is called the conjugate of the other. If it is known that 
b 0, then (a + bi) and (a — bi) are called conjugate imaginary numbers: 
they differ only in the signs of their imaginary parts.
A quotient of two complex numbers can be expressed in the form 
{a + bi) by multiplying both numerator and denominator by the con­
jugate of the denominator, if necessary.

109. Geometrical representation of complex numbers
On a coordinate system, as in Figure 91, we may represent any com­
plex number (a + bi) by the point P:(a, b). P falls on OX when and only 
when b = 0 and hence (a + bi) is a real number; therefore, we call OX 
the axis of real numbers. Similarly, (a + bi) falls on OF when and only 
when a = 0 and hence (a + bi) is a pure 
imaginary number or zero. Therefore, 
we call OY the axis of pure imaginaries.
Illustration 1. In Figure 91, A repre­
sents (— 3 + 6i); B represents (— 5 + 0 • i), 
or — 5; C represents — 3i.
It is sometimes useful to think of the
■■ >
vector OP, instead of merely the point P, 
as representing (a + bi).

Note 1. Imaginary numbers were introduced in the 16th century by 
Cardan. The words real and imaginary, as now employed in references to 
numbers, were introduced by Descartes (1637), and the symbol i for V — 1 by 
Euler (1748). A Norwegian surveyor, Wessel (1797), was the first to employ 
the geometrical representation of complex numbers on a plane.

111. Trigonometric form of a complex number
In Figure 94, P represents any complex number z = a + bi, and the 
units of length on the axes are the same. Let 
r = OP, which will be positive or zero in all cases, 
and is called the radius vector for P. Let 6 be any 
angle whose initial side is OX and terminal side is 
OP. Then,
r = Va2 + fe2,

a = r cos 9, b = r sin 9, 
(2)

(3)

(4)

COMPLEX NUMBERS
where (3) does not apply when a = 0. We call r(cos 0 + i sin 0) the 
trigonometric form (or polar form) of z; the number r is called the absolute 
value (or modulus) of z, and 0 is called the amplitude (or argument) of z. 
If 0 is one amplitude, the other permissible amplitudes are 0 + k • 360°, 
where k may be any integer. In writing a trigonometric form for z, unless 
otherwise necessary we choose the smallest permissible positive or zero 
value for the amplitude.
Illustration 1. If 20° is one amplitude, others are 380°, — 340°, etc. 
Thus,
5(cos 20° + i sin 20°) = 5(cos 380° + i sin 380°) = etc.
Illustration 2. The complex number 0 can be written in the form 
0 ■ (cos 8 + i sin 0) where 0 has any value; the absolute value of 0 is 0.
If two complex numbers are equal, they are represented by the same 
point in Figure 94, and hence have the same absolute value, and ampli­
tudes which are equal or differ merely by some integral multiple of 360°.
Illustration 3. To change from the polar form to the form (a + bi), we 
use trigonometric tables. Thus, by use of Table IV,
6(cos 35° + i sin 35°) = 6(.819 + .574f) = 4.914 + 3.444f.
To express a number (a + bi) in trigonometric form, plot (a + bi), 
notice the quadrant for 0, and then find r, 0 from (1), (2), (3).

Products in trigonometric form
The amplitude of a product of complex numbers is the sum of their 
amplitudes and the absolute value of the product is the product of the 
absolute values of the factors.
Proof. Consider a product of just two complex numbers:
n(cos 0i + i sin 0f) ■ r2(cos 02 + i sin 02)
= rir2(cos 0i cos 02 + i sin 0i cos 02 + i cos 0i sin 02 + i2 sin 0i sin 02)
= nr2[(cos 0i cos 02 — sin 0i sin 02) + z(sin 0i cos 02 + cos 0i sin 02)].
Hence, by the addition formulas (I) and (II) of Chapter Eight,
ri(cos + i sin 9i) ■ r2(cos 92 + i sin 02)
= rir2[cos (0i + 02) + i sin (0i + 02)].

The nth roots of a complex number
If n is a positive integer, to say that p is an nth root of a number H 
means that H = pn. Thus, if p is a cube root of H, we have H = p3.

APPENDIX
FOR PLANE TRIGONOMETRY
NOTE 1
Accuracy of Computation
By illustrations, we could verify that the following rules do not 
underestimate the accuracy of computation. Also, it must be admitted 
that the rules sometimes overestimate the accuracy. We adopt these rules 
for computation in this text.
I. In adding approximate values, round off the result in the first place 
where the last significant digit of any given value is found.
II. In multiplying or dividing approximate values, round off the result 
to the smallest number of significant figures found in any given value.
Illustration 1. Let a = 35.64, b = 342.72, and c = .03147 be approxi­
mate values. Then, a + b + c is not reliable beyond the second decimal place 
because both a and b are subject to an unknown error which may be as large 
as 5 units in the third decimal place. Hence, we write 
a + b + c = 378.39147 = 378.39, approximately.
Illustration 2. If x = 31.27 and y = .021 are approximate values, we 
state that xy = .66, because y has only two significant digits:
xy = 31.27(.O21) = .65667 = .66, approximately.
Illustration 3. If a surveyor measures a rectangular field as 385.6' by 
432.4', it would give an unjustified appearance of accuracy to write that the 
area is
(385.6) (432.4) = 166,733.44 square feet.
For, an error of .05 foot in either dimension would cause an error of about 20 
square feet in the area. By Rule II, a reasonably justified result would be that 
the area is 166,700 square feet, to the nearest 100 square feet.
In problems where approximate values enter, or where approximate 
results are obtained from exact data, the results should be rounded off 
so as to avoid giving a false appearance of accuracy. No hard and fast 
rules for such rounding off should be adopted, and the final decision as 
to the accuracy of a result should be made only after a careful examina­
tion of the details of the solution.

A Logarithmic Scale
In Figure 96, after selecting the unit of length, HK, we lay off a scale 
above the line in the usual way. Below the line, each value of x is placed 
at the point under the value of X which equals log x (where “ log ” means 
“logio”); that is, if x is under X, then log x = X.

If we blot out the upper numbers in Figure 96, we obtain the scale in 
Figure 97, called a logarithmic scale. For contrast, the upper scale in 
Figure 96 may be called a uniform scale. On a logarithmic scale, if the 
unit of distance is properly chosen, the distance from x = 1 to x = N equals 
log N. Since N has no real logarithm if N 0, we find neither zero nor 
negative numbers on a logarithmic scale.

Note 1. Logarithmic scales are employed on the various scales on a slide 
rule.
*
 The usual 10-inch slide rule permits rapid computation with about the 
accuracy obtained by using three-place logarithms. The longer the slide rule, 
the greater is the accuracy obtainable by using it. The fundamental principle 
involved in the primary use of a slide rule is illustrated in Figure 98. Scales A 
and B are logarithmic. To find, for instance, the product 3 X 4, we place the 
1 of scale B under the 3 of A. Then, above the 4 of B we read the product 
3 X 4 on A. We obtain this result because
OK = OH + HK = log 3 + log 4 = log (3 X 4).

NOTE 3
Logarithms of Functions of Angles Near 0° and 90°
The tabular differences increase rapidly in Table IX as we approach 
0° in the columns headed log sin, log tan, and log cot. Hence, if a log­
arithm is determined by interpolation in these columns, the error is 
extremely large on page 36 of Table IX and remains undesirably large 
(> .00001) until we reach page 40. Table X is provided for avoiding 
these large errors of interpolation. In Table X, we let
sin a = s and
tan a t 
M ~ 
M ~
where M is the number of minutes in at. Then, 
sin a = sM and tan a = tM;
log sin a = log s + log M; 
log tan a = log t + log M.
If we let S = log s and T = log t, then
log sin a = S + log M; log tan a - T + log M. 
(1)
3 and T are computed by the methods of advanced mathematics; the 
values of S and T are tabulated in Table Xa for a between 0° and 3°.
I. To find log sin a, log tan a, or log cot a for an acute angle a near 0°:
1. Express a in minutes and decimal parts of a minute, to find M.
2. Find log M from Table VIII and S and T from Table Xa.
3. Use (1) to find log sin a and log tan a; to find log cot a, recall
cot a = ———, or log cot a = — log tan a. 
tan a
II. To find log cos j3, log tan fl, or log cot fl for an acute angle fl 
near 90°, let a = 90° — /3. Recall cos /3 = sin a, tan /3 = cot a, 
cot fl = tan a; then use (I).
III. To find an acute angle a near 0° if log sin a (or log tan a) is given:
1. Find a to the nearest minute by inspection of Table IX.
2. Find S (or T) from Table Xa; substitute for S and log sin a in (1), 
and find log M; then obtain M from Table VIII.
IV. To find an acute angle fl near 90° if log cos /?, log tan /3, or log cot 0 
is given, write fl = 90° — a, and first find a by use of (III).

From Table VIII, M = 158.44', or a = 2° 38.44' = 2° 38' 26".

provided by Table Xb, for angles very near 0°. If angles are expressed only 
to the nearest .1', Table Xb demands no interpolation.
Note 3. A slowly changing function of an unknown angle a offers a poor 
means for determining a. Hence, when possible, we avoid using cos a in de­
termining a if a is near 0°, or sin ol if a is near 90°.
NOTE 4
Plane Surveying
At first, let us assume that all points involved are in the horizontal 
plane. Then, a line segment from M to P is referred to as course 
MP. The north-south projection of MP is called its latitude, and is 
considered positive or negative according as P is north or south of M. The 
east-west projection of MP is called its departure and is labeled posi­
tive or negative according as P is east or west 
of M. Then, from Figure 99, if is the 
acute bearing angle of the direction MP, 
and MP is the length of MP,
latitude = ± MP cos <3; 
departure = ± MP sin fi;

Proofs of the General Reduction Formulas
Let us suppose that, by the method of page 72, the reduction formu­
las have already been proved for functions of (90° + 6), (90° — 0), 
0 — 90°), and (0 — 90°). These results justify the following statement.
I. The addition of 90° or — 90° to 0 or — 0 gives an angle where each 
of its functions is numerically equal to the cofunction of 0.
By successive applications of (I), we may then prove (1) and (2) of 
page 75.

To obtain a formula for any function of (n • 90° ± 0), where n is a j 
positive integer, we could apply statement (I) in subtracting 90° a total ] 
of n times. Each of these steps would give a result changing from a | 
function to the cofunction. If n is even, these n changes conclude with 1 
the original named function for 0, multiplied by ± 1. If n is odd, the.] 
conclusion involves the cofunction of the original named function for 0, | 
multiplied by ± 1. That is, we have proved the following result for the I

of — 0. Thus, (1) is true for all integers n.

NOTE 8
Use of Reversible Operations in Proving an Identity
A trigonometric identity may sometimes be established as follows:
1. Assume that the conjectured identity is true and manipulate it until 
an obvious identity is obtained.
2. Start anew with this final obvious identity and prove that, from it, 
the conjectured identity can be obtained.
The action described in Part 2 usually amounts to verifying that 
each step involved in Part 1 can be reversed, to lead us from the obvious 
identity back to the conjectured identity. A by-product of the method ; 
is that, if Part 1 does not lead to an obvious identity, we may obtain 
some equality which we recognize as a conditional equation. Then, we 
might conclude that the conjectured identity is a conditional equation, 
and incidentally have a means for its solution. If the present method ■ 
is to be used, it is a prime necessity that the reversibility of each step be 
examined and commented upon, as an essential part of the proof.
1 + cos x _ sin a:
sin x 1 — cos x '

NOTE 9
General Proof of the Addition Formulas
Suppose that the following formulas have been proved geometrically 
in case a and /3 are on the range 0° to 90°, inclusive:
sin (a + /3) = sin a cos /3 + cos a sin /3; 
(1)
cos (a + /?) = cos a cos /3 — sin a sin /?. 
(2)
Theorem I. If a and ft are angles for which (1) and (2) are true,
then they remain true if either a or ft is increased or decreased by 90°.
Proof. 1. Let a' = a + 90°. Then sin a’ = cos a; cos a! = — sin a.
2. 
sin (a' + ft) = sin E(a + ft) + 90°] = cos (a + ft)
|_using (2)] 
= cos a cos ft - sin a sin ft. 
(3)
3. 
Replace a by a! on the right in (1) and use Step 1:
sin a' cos ft + cos a' sin ft = cos a cos ft — sin a sin ft. 
(4)
We see that (3) and the right-hand side of (4) are identical. Hence
sin (a' + ft) = sin a' cos ft + cos a' sin ft. 
(5)
Since a' = a + 90°, (5) states that (1) is true if a is increased by 90°.
4. 
Similarly, by starting with a' = a — 90°, it can be proved that (1) 
is true if a is decreased by 90°. Similar facts can be proved about (2). 
To prove the theorem as it applies to ft, we would similarly introduce ft' by 
ft' = 3 + 90° or ft’ = ft — Q0°.
Theorem II. Formulas (1) and (2) hold for all values of a and ft.
Proof. 1. Let y and 0 be any angles not on the range 0° to 90°, in­
clusive. Then, there exist angles a and ft on the range 0° to 90°, inclusive, 
such that y = a + m • 90° and 0 = ft + n • 90°, where m and n are ap­
propriate integers. We know that (1) and (2) are true for a and ft.

174
APPENDIX
2. We can pass from a to y through a sequence of angles, by successive! 
additions of 90°, if m > 0, or subtractions of 90°, if m < 0. By Theorem I, 3 
formulas (1) and (2) remain true if a is replaced in turn by the successive 1 
angles of this sequence. Hence, finally, we may replace a by y in (1) and I 
(2). Similarly, we find that we may replace 0 by 0 in (1) and (2). Thus, i 
(1) and (2) are true if a and 0 are replaced by y and 9, respectively. 1

SPHERICAL TRIGONOMETRY


TWELVE
RIGHT SPHERICAL TRIANGLES
116. Results From the geometry of a sphere
A sphere is a closed surface which is the locus of all points equidistant 
from a point called the center of the sphere. On a sphere, let A, B, C be 
three points which are not on a great circle of the sphere. Then, if 
A, B, C are joined by arcs of great circles, the figure ABC is called 
a spherical triangle whose sides are the arcs AB, BC, and AC, and 
whose angles are the angles of intersection of these arcs. Spherical 
trigonometry is concerned with the development and application of 
trigonometric formulas involving the parts of a spherical triangle. The 
following facts concerning spherical geometry should be recalled.
A great circle of a sphere is its intersection with a plane through the 
center of the sphere, and has the same center and radius as the sphere.
The poles of a great circle of a sphere are the points P and P' in which 
a line through the center of the circle, perpendicular to its plane, inter­
sects the sphere. If A is any point on the sphere, we can construct just 
one great circle having A as a pole.
If A and B are any two points on a sphere which are not diametrically 
opposite with respect to its center, we can construct just one great circle 
passing through A and B.
The angle of intersection BA C of two arcs 
AB and AC of great circles is defined as the 
angle TAM formed by the tangents A T and 
AM to these arcs at A. In Figure 105, where 
0 is the center of the sphere, notice that A T 
and AM are perpendicular to AA', the in­
tersection of planes A'OAB and A'OAC. 
This angle TAM also measures the dihedral 
angle formed by the planes A'OAB and 
A'OAC in which the arcs lie.
The angular measure of an arc of a great circle is the measure of 
the angle subtended by the arc at the center of the sphere. The length 
177

178
RIGHT SPHERICAL TRIANGLES
of the arc can be found if we know its angular measure and the radius 
of the sphere.
117. Theorems concerning spherical triangles
Hereafter, we shall measure arcs of great circles in angular units. 
Therefore, both the sides and the angles of any spherical triangle will be 
described in angular units. Moreover, we agree to consider only spherical 
triangles each of whose sides and angles is less than 180°. As a standard 
notation for a spherical triangle ABC, we shall let a, 13, and y represent 
the angles at A, B, and C, respectively, and let a, b, and c be the corre­
sponding opposite sides. The letter 
0 will always represent the center of 
the sphere on which triangle ABC 
lies.
Corresponding to any spherical 
triangle ABC, as in Figure 106, we 
can construct a trihedral angle 
0-ABC whose dihedral angles meas­
ure the angles and whose face angles 
AOB, COB, and CO A measure the 
sides of the triangle ABC.
Illustration 1. In Figure 106, the sides of AABC lie in the planes OAB, ’ 
OBC, and OAC, which form the faces of the trihedral angle 0-ABC. From 1 
Section 116, /ABC or ft has the same measure as the dihedral angle whose J 
edge is OB and whose faces are planes OAB and OBC. Also, we verify that 
ZAOC measures arc AC; that is,
b = /AOC; c = /AOB; a = /BOC.
The following theorems from geometry will be useful.
I. The sum of the sides of a spherical triangle is less than 360°. A
II. The sum of the angles of a spherical triangle is greater than 180° i 
and less than 540°. 
i
MM
*
Definition I. The spherical excess of a spherical triangle is the | 
amount by which the sum of the angles of the triangle exceeds 180°. 
|
Two great circles on a sphere intersect to divide the sphere into four I 
parts each of which is called a lune of the sphere. Any third great circle, J 
not passing through the points of intersection of the first two circles, I 
divides each lune into two spherical triangles. Hence, in general, three 1 
great circles on a sphere divide it into eight spherical triangles. 
|
Note 1. If a spherical triangle ABC has a side or an angle greater than ] 
180°, various arcs of the great circles which intersect to form triangle ABC 1 
also form related triangles each of whose sides and angles is less than 180°. I
fl

RIGHT SPHERICAL TRIANGLES 
179
It can be verified later that any of the typical problems concerning the given 
triangle may be solved by investigating some one of these related triangles. 
Hence, our agreement to consider only those triangles whose sides and angles 
are all less than 180° is not an essential restriction on our results.
118. 
Polar triangles
If ABC is a spherical triangle, we can construct three great circles 
having A, B, and C, respectively, as poles. These circles intersect to 
form eight triangles, among which there is just one which may be lettered 
A'B'C, as in Figure 107, so that
1. A, B, and C are, respectively, poles of the circles on which B'C, 
A'C', and A'B' lie, and
2. A and A' are on the same side of  B'C; B and B' are on the same 
side of A'C; C and C are on the same side of A'B'.
*
We call triangle A'B'C the polar triangle of ABC.—In geometry, the 
following theorems are proved about polar triangles.
I. If A'B’C is the polar triangle of ABC, then ABC is the polar triangle 
of A'B'C.
II. Each side of triangle ABC is the supplement of the corresponding 
angle of the polar triangle A'B'C; each angle of ABC is the supple­
ment of the corresponding side of A'B’C.

Note 1. A moderate modification of the preceding proof, with Figure 108 
altered, would establish (I) to (X) when one or both of the conditions a < 90°, 
d < 90° are not satisfied. It will be found that (I) to (V) inclusive are sufficient 
for most purposes. (I) and (III) may be remembered by analogy with cor­
responding formulas for right plane triangles.
120. Rules for species
We shall say that two angular magnitudes are of the same or of 
different species according as they terminate in the same or in different 
quadrants. In this statement the angles are thought of in their standard 
positions on a coordinate system.
Illustration 1. 130° and 75° are of different species.
The following theorems are useful in discussing the species of parts 
of a spherical triangle. Theorems I and II are proved in texts on solid 
geometry.
I. The sum of two sides of a spherical triangle is greater than the third 
side.
II. If two sides of a spherical triangle are unequal, the angles opposite 
are unequal and the greater angle is opposite the greater side. If two 
sides are equal, the angles opposite are equal.
III. In a right spherical triangle ABC,
1. a and at, are of the same species;
2. b and are of the same species;
3. if c < 90°, then a and b are of the same species;
4. if c > 90°, then a and b are of different species.

182
RIGHT SPHERICAL TRIANGLES
Proof. From (VII) on page 180, tan a = sin b tan a. Since b < 180° < 
then sin b > 0. Hence, tan a and tan a are both positive and a and a are] 
both less than 90°, or tan a and tan a are both negative and a and a are
*
 
both between 90° and 180°. Thus, a and a. are of the same species. (Parts ] 
2, 3, and 4 will be proved by the student.) 
j
* We shall find it possible to solve any right spherical triangle by use of only 
formulas (I) to (V) on page 180.

122. Isosceles and quadrantal triangles
Suppose that the spherical triangle ABC is isosceles, with a = b; then 
it follows that a = /3- If a great circle is passed through 
C and the midpoint D of AB, as in Figure 110, triangles 
ADC and BDC are symmetric right triangles with 
right angles at D. By first solving one of these right 
triangles, we can solve triangle ABC if any two of 
its distinct parts (a, a, c, 7) are given.
Illustration 1. In Figure 110, if we are given 
a = 750, b = 75°, and 7 = 58°, then A ACT) = 29°. 
Hence, we can find a and AD on solving AADC by use of 
(I) to (V). Then c = AB = 2(AD).
A quadrantal triangle ABC is a triangle with at least one side equal 
to 90°; suppose that the triangle is lettered so that c = 90°. Then, from 
(1) on page 179, in the polar triangle A'B'C, 7' = 180° — 90° = 90°. 
Thus, A'B'C is a right spherical triangle. Hence, if we are given two 
parts of a quadrantal triangle in addition to the 90° side, we can solve 
the triangle by first solving the polar triangle and then using (1) on 
page 179.
Example 1. Solve AABC if c = 90°, 7 = 43°, and a = 132°.
Solution. 1. In the polar AA'B'C,
y’ = 180° - c = 90°; c' = 180° - 7 = 137°; a' = 180° - a = 48°.
2. On solving AA'B'C we obtain a' = 30° 27'; b' = 148° 2'; 13' = 129° 5'. 
Hence, from (1) on page 179,
a = 180° - a' = 149° 33'; 
0 = 180° - b' = 31° 58'; b = 50° 55'.

123. Napier’s rules
In the examples in this chapter, it has been assumed that formulas 
(I) to (V) have been memorized as a basis. However, if a student pre­
fers, he may use all of (I) to (X). In such a case, it would be convenient 
for him to recall these formulas through rules formulated by Napier, the 
inventor of logarithms. To describe his method, we let Co-a equal 
(90° — a), and introduce Co-fl and Co-c similarly. Then, we shall call 
(a, b, Co-a, Co-c, Co-fl) the five circular parts for the triangle ABC. We
shall think of these parts ar­
ranged around a circle or 
around the triangle ABC in the 
order (a, b, a, c, fl). Thus, to 
each part there correspond two 
adjacent parts and two opposite 
parts. Then, if each of the fol­
lowing rules is applied to each 
circular part, we obtain for­
mulas (I) to (X).
Rule 1. The sine of any circular part is equal to the product of the
tangents of the two adjacent parts.
Rule 2. The sine of any circular part is equal to the product of the J 
cosines of the two opposite parts. 
1
In applying these rules, recall that, for any angle 0, any function ’ 
of (90° — &) is equal to the co-function of 0. 
j
Illustration 1. The parts adjacent to Co-fl are Co-c and a; the parts I
opposite to Co-fl are Co-a and b. Hence, from Rules 1 and 2,
sin (Co-fl) = tan (Co-c) tan a; 
or 
cos fl = cot c tan a; 
(1)
sin (Co-fl) = cos (Co-a) cos b; 
or 
cos fl = sin a cos b. 
(2)
We verify that (1) is equivalent to (VIII) and (2) is (IV).
By Napier’s rules, we can write a formula connecting any three of 
the parts (a, b, a, fl, c) without referring to formulas (I) to (X).
Illustration 2. To write a formula connecting a, fl, and a, we first notice 
that Co-fl and a are opposite to Co-a. Hence, by use of Rule 2, 
sin (Co-a) = cos (Co-fl) cos a; or cos a = sin fl cos a.
*Only five-place results are given.

In any spherical triangle ABC, the sines of the sides are proportional 
to the sines of the corresponding opposite angles. That is,

127. 
Solution of spherical triangles
The following cases arise in solving spherical triangles.
*
I. Given three sides.
II. Given three angles.
III. Given two sides and the included angle.
IV. Given two angles and the included side.
V. Given two sides and an angle opposite one of these sides.
VI. Given two angles and a side opposite one of these angles.
* 
Hereafter, unless otherwise stated, any triangle referred to will be a spherical 
triangle.

190
OBLIQUE SPHERICAL TRIANGLES
If all angles of AABC are given (Case II), by use of (1) on page 170 
we can find all sides of the polar AA'B'C". Then, we could solve for 
the angles of AA'jB'C", which is a problem under Case I. From these 
angles, we could then find the sides of AABC. Thus, Case II for ATBC 
could be solved under Case I for £\A'B'C. Similarly, Cases IV and VI 
for A. ABC could be solved under Cases III and V, respectively, applied 
to AA/B'C'. Usually we shall not bring AA'B'C into the discussion. 
Instead, available formulas for AABC will apply directly to Cases II, 
IV, and VI.
128. Rules for species
If an angle 0 
90° is known to lie between 0° and 180°, and sin 0
is given, we are aware that two values of 0 correspond to the data. Thus, 
if the law of sines is used to find an unknown side or angle of a spherical 
triangle, in general two values will be obtained for the unknown part. 
Then, it becomes necessary to decide whether neither value, one of the 
values, or both values can be used for a solution. In making such a 
decision, or in grouping values obtained otherwise for various parts of 
a triangle, we usually employ Theorem II of page 181, or the rules for 
species applying to a right triangle.
Note 1. If an unknown angle 0 
90° between 0° and 180° is being found
by use of tan 0, cos 0, or hav 0, then just one value for 0 is obtained. This causes 
us to use tan 0, cos 0, or hav 0 sometimes to remove an ambiguity resulting 
from previous use of sin 0.
★Note 2. The following rules for species are sometimes useful, 
rules can be proved by use of later formulas.
These
Rule 1. Half the sum of any two sides is of the same species as half the 
sum of the opposite angles.
Rule 2. A side {or angle) which differs from 90° by more than another side 
(or angle) has the same species as the angle (or side) opposite the first side 
(or angle).

FOURTEEN
APPLICATIONS OF SPHERICAL TRIANGLES
137. Latitude and longitude
For our purposes, no appreciable error is made in assuming that the 
earth’s surface is a sphere with radius 3959 miles. The earth revolves 
on a diameter whose intersections with the surface are called the north 
pole, N, and the south pole, S. If an observer stands at A on the earth, 
the direction of N is called north and of <S is called south; if he faces north, 
the perpendicular direction to the right is called east and the opposite 
direction is called west. The equator is the great circle on the earth whose 
poles are N and S. The meridian of a point A on the earth is the semi­
circle NAS of the great circle 
through N, A, and S, as in Figure 
116. The latitude of A is the angu­
lar measure of the arc of the merid­
ian of A extending from the equator 
to A. If 0 is the earth’s center and 
L is the latitude of A, then, in 
Figure 116, L = AHO A. Latitude 
is measured from 0° to 90°, north 
or south of the equator. The co­
latitude of A is the complement of 
its latitude and is the angular 
measure of arc AN, in Figure 116.
Illustration 1. For A in Fig­
ure 116, L = 40° N and the colatitude 
is 50°, approximately.
All points on the earth with the same latitude L lie on a small circle, 
with N and S as poles, in a plane parallel to the equator. This small 
circle is called a parallel of latitude. In Figure 116, the circle AUV is 
the parallel of latitude through A.
The longitude of a point A on the earth is the angle of intersection, 
X, of the meridian of A and the prime meridian of Greenwich,
*
 England,
* Used by many nations as the zero meridian for longitude.
201

202 
APPLICATIONS OF SPHERICAL TRIANGLES
indicated by G in Figure 116. Longitude is measured from 0° to 180°, 
inclusive, east or west from Greenwich. The longitude X is the measure 
of the smallest angle formed by the planes ONA and ONG', or, in Figure 
116, X = A KOH in the plane of the equator.
Illustration 2. In Figure 116, the longitude of A is approximately 
100° W, meaning west of Greenwich.
Any point A on the earth is definitely located by specifying two 
angular coordinates for A, its latitude L and longitude X.
138. Nautical miles
A nautical mile is defined as the length of the arc which is subtended 
on a great circle on the earth by an angle of 1' at the center of the circle:
(1' of arc on a great circle on the earth) = 1 nautical mile; 
(1)
1 nautical mile = 6080 feet = 1.1515 statute miles. 
(2)
To prove (2), we compute the circumference of a great circle on the 
earth and divide this by the number of minutes in 360°:
360° = (360) (60') = 21,600 minutes.
(circumference of a great circle) = 2tt(3959) statute miles.
2tf(3959) 
.,
1 nautical mile = Q | nn statute miles = 1.152 statute miles.
139. Directions on the earth
The horizontal plane at A on the earth is an ideal plane tangent to 
the earth at A.—This plane consists of all lines tangent to the earth at A.
Each direction from A on the earth corresponds to a direction arrow 
tangent to the earth at A; the arrow radiates from A m the horizontal 
plane. The directions N, S, E, W correspond to the arrows AN, AS, A 
AW, respectively, in the horizontal plane represented in Figure 117.
In referring to a path AB on the earth, 
we shall mean a directed path,
*
 from the first 
point A to the second point B. At A, there 
is a line tangent to AB, and we direct this tan­
gent from A toward B. The direction of the 
corresponding arrow AB in the horizontal 
plane, as in Figure 117, is called the direction 
of the path AB at A. The bearing of a point B 
from A is defined as the direction of the 
shortest great-circle path from A to B.
In the horizontal plane, as in Figure 117, we shall describe any direc-
* Assumed to be so “smooth” that it has a tangent line at each point.
B
W
Fig. 117

APPLICATIONS OF SPHERICAL TRIANGLES 
203
tion AB, indicated as an arrow, by specifying its azimuth. Recall that 
this is defined as the positive measure of the angle y between the north 
direction AN and AB, measured to the east (clockwise) from the north. 
The direction of the track of a ship or airplane at any point A on the 
track is called the course of the ship or airplane, or the course of the 
track, at A. Thus, a course or any other direction is an azimuth, which 
is an angle on the range 0° to 360°, including 0° but not 360°.
Illustbation 1. If a ship sails on course 250°, indicated by AB in Figure 
117, the track is in a southwesterly direction. The direction of AB is 70° west 
of south.
140. Great-circle distances
The shortest distance between points A and B on the earth is the 
length of the shortest arc AB of the great circle through A and B. This 
shortest distance is called the great-circle distance between A and B, and
is on the range 0° to 180°, inclusive, 
est in geography and navigation. 
circle navigation
*
 for a ship or an 
airplane is the determination of the 
great-circle distance between given 
points A and B, and the course of 
the great-circle track AB at any 
point, particularly at A and at B.
Illustration 1. In Figure 118, 
the course of AB at A is about 130°, 
..nd is called the initial course of AB.— 
If D is any point on AB, and ND is 
the arc of the longitude circle through 
D, the course of AB at D is /NDB. 
The course changes continuously as 
we move from A to B. At B, the 
final course is (180° — Z ABN).
Great-circle distances are of inter-

Many problems of great-circle navigation are solved by use of triangle 
NAB in Figure 118. If G represents Greenwich, /GN A and /GNB are 
the longitudes of A and B; /BNA is found by subtraction if the longi­
tudes are given. In Figure 118,
AN = colatitude of A ;
BN = 90° + (latitude of B), 
since B is south of the equator. The angular measure of a great-circle 
arc AB can be translated into linear distance by recalling that 1' of arc 
on a great circle is 1 nautical mile in length.

141. The celestial sphere
In the sky, we see the heavenly bodies as though they were on a 
sphere with our observation point as the center. This celestial sphere may 
be thought of as so large that, by comparison, the earth’s radius is of 
negligible length. Hence, we refer to the earth as the center of the sphere. 
The celestial north pole P and south pole P' are the points where the 
earth’s axis, extended through the earth’s north and south poles, re­
spectively, meets the celestial sphere. To any observer, this sphere 
appears to rotate from east to west about the axis PP', because the earth 
rotates from west to east about its axis.
Note 1. Any great circle referred to at present will be on the celestial 
sphere.
By the position of a celestial object, we mean the point where the 
line of sight from an observer to the object meets the celestial sphere. 
To discuss the positions of celestial objects, we introduce the following 
astronomical terms, illustrated in Figure 119 on page 206.
The celestial equator is the great circle whose poles are P and P', 
and is the intersection of the plane of the earth’s equator and the celestial 
sphere.
At any instant, the zenith of an observer is the point Z vertically 
above him on the celestial sphere; the point Z' diametrically opposite 
to Z is called the nadir.
The horizon of an observer is the great circle whose poles are Z and Z’. 
The plane of the horizon is tangent to the earth’s surface at the observer’s 
position, and is called his horizontal plane.
The celestial meridian is the great circle through the zenith Z and 
the north pole P. The intersections N and S of this meridian and the

206
APPLICATIONS OF SPHERICAL TRIANGLES
horizon are called the north point and south point, respectively, where N 
is the intersection nearest to P. If the observer faces N, the points E 
and W to his right and left on the horizon are called the east point and 
west point, respectively. The equator and the horizon intersect at 
E and W.
The hour circle of a point A on the celestial sphere is the great circle
through P and A.
The hour angle
*
 of A is the angle ZPA between the meridian and 
the hour circle of A, measured east or west from the meridian from 0° Mt 
to 180°, inclusive. This angle is measured in hours or in degrees, where 
one hour equals 15°. 
"U
Note 2. One minute in the hour system is o th of 15°, or 15' in usual measure ; 
one second in the hour system is 15". The hour system of measurement, proves 
useful because the celestial sphere rotates through 15° in one sidereal hour, 
which is about 10" less than an ordinary solar hour.
The declination of A is the angular distance of A from the equator, 
measured along the hour circle of A. The declination, DA in Figure 119, 
is designated as positive or negative according as A is north or south of 
the equator.
The vertical circle of A is the great circle through Z and A.
The altitude of A is the angular distance of A from the horizon, 
measured along the vertical circle of A. The altitude, TA in Figure 119. 
is designated as positive or negative according as A is above or below the 
horizon.
* More properly, the local hour angle, as distinguished from the Greenwich and j 
sidereal hour angles.

r
APPLICATIONS OF SPHERICAL TRIANGLES 
207
The azimuth of A is the angle NZA between the meridian and the 
vertical circle of A, counted from 0° to 360°, including 0° but excluding 
360°, east (clockwise) from the north point. In Figure 119, arc NET 
measures the azimuth of A.
We notice that the altitude, azimuth, and hour angle of A depend 
not only on the position of A on the celestial sphere but also on the 
time of observation and the position of the observer on the earth. The 
declination depends only on the position of A on the celestial sphere.
At any instant, the position of A on the celestial sphere is uniquely 
determined with respect to an observer if the altitude and azimuth are 
known. This means of locating A can be referred to as the altitude­
azimuth coordinate system. Also, the position of A can be described by 
specifying its hour angle and declination; this means is called the declina­
tion-hour-angle system of coordinates.
*
 Either system corresponds in its 
essential nature to the latitude-longitude system for indicating positions 
on the earth.
142. Location of the celestial north polef
The altitude of the celestial north pole is the same as the latitude of the 
observer.
Proof. Consider the plane section of the earth, 
in Figure 120, where O is the observer, T is the 
earth’s north pole, and T' is the earth’s south 
pole. The reader should imagine himself stand­
ing with his feet at O and his head in the direc­
tion OZ. The arrows OZ, OP, and ON point at 
the zenith, north pole, and north point, respec­
tively, on the celestial sphere. We verify that 0 
is the altitude of P. In Figure 120, we notice 
that ON is perpendicular to one side of angle L 
and OP to the other. Hence 0 = L, where L is 
the latitude of O.
143. The astronomical triangle
At any instant, for an observer at a given point on the earth, the 
astronomical triangle corresponding to a specified object A or point A 
on the celestial sphere is the spherical triangle ZPA of Figure 119, where
* Another coordinate (see texts on astronomy and navigation), called the right 
ascension of A, is paired with the declination to give a third system of coordinates, 
which is ven- important in astronomy and navigation.
f In Figure 119, associated remarks, and certain later formulas involving the 
pole P, we assume that the observer is in the northern hemisphere. Similar details 
relating to the pole P’ would apply for an observer in the southern hemisphere.

208 
APPLICATIONS OF SPHERICAL TRIANGLES
ZP = 90° — (altitude of P) = colatitude of 0;
AZ = 90° — (altitude of A) = coaltitude of A;
AP = 90° — (declination of A) = codeclination of A;
ZZPA = hour angle of A (east or west of meridian);
f — azimuth, if A is east of meridian;
Z PZA s
[ = 360 — azimuth, if A is west of meridian.
Note 1. Problems associated with the astronomical triangle are important 
in navigation as well as in astronomy. It should not be assumed, however, 
that a navigator always carries out the trigonometric solution of spherical 
triangles as required in the exercises. Excellent navigation tables and special 
computing instruments enable him to eliminate much of the computation.
Note 2. The declination of the Sun, certain planets, and prominent stars 
at all times during each year are available in American navigation tables. 
Also, when an observer’s longitude is known, these tables give him the local 
hour angle of any one of these celestial bodies at any time.
For any observer, solar noon is the instant when the sun is on arc PZS 
of the meridian. Any time Ivliich we mention will be local solar time, 
specified as before or after solar noon. In Figure 119, if A represents the 
sun at t hours before noon, then ZZPA = t hours; at t hours after noon, 
A is in the west and again ZZPA = t hours.
144. Applications involving the astronomical triangle
I. To find the time of day (solar time') of an observer whose latitude is 
known, when the sun’s declination and altitude are given.
In Problem I, the declination of the sun would be given by a nautical 
almanac and the altitude would be measured with a sextant. In the 
astronomical triangle ZPA, with A as the sun, all sides are given. Hence, 
the solution comes under Case I for spherical triangles. The results 
may be found by use of haversine formulas, as in Section 129 on page 190, 
or by the methods of Section 133 on page 194.
Example 1. Find the solar time in New York (L = 40° 50' N) at 
an instant in a morning when the sun’s declination is 16° 30' and the 
sun’s altitude is observed as 35° 20'.
Solution. 1. In AZP A in Figure 119, where A is the sun,
ZP = 49° 10', ZA = 54° 40', AP = 73° 30'.

in standard notation, (1) becomes
a = 49° 10', b = 73° 30', c = 54° 40'. 
(2)
We desire only the value of y = Z ZPA in the triangle.

observation at 3 hr. and 49 min. before noon, or 8:11 a.m.

Comment. We could also find y by use of (IX), page 192.
A navigator at sea or in an airplane can use a result like that in 
Example 1 to determine his longitude, if he knows the solar time of 
Greenwich at the instant of observation. Greenwich time might be ob­
tained by radio. Then, the difference in time would permit computation 
of the observer’s longitude.
II. To find the solar time of sunrise or sunset in a given latitude when 
the declination of the sun is known (from an ephemeris).
Any instance of Problem II is a special case of Problem I, with the 
altitude of the sun as 0°. Then, in triangle ZPA, ZA = 90° so that the 
triangle is quadrantal. The solution can still be obtained as in Example 1, 
but the method of page 185 also would apply.
III. In a given latitude, to find the altitude and azimuth of a celestial 
object whose declination and hour angle are known.
•kN ole 1. The preceding statement of Problem III is somewhat unnatural 
because no nautical almanac is available. In navigation, where Problem III 
is met, the data would consist of the observer’s latitude and longitude and the 
name of a star A, whose altitude and azimuth are to be found. Then, a nautical 
almanac would give the declination, and also the hour angle for A because the 
longitude of the observer is known. Problem III arises when a navigator wishes 
to determine his latitude and longitude by observing the altitude and azimuth 
for each of two stars, Ai and A2. He starts by estimating his present latitude 
and longitude roughly on the basis of past information. Then, as in Problem III, 
he computes for each of Ai and A2 the altitude and azimuth which the star 
would have at the estimated position 0 corresponding to the assumed latitude 
and longitude. Also, the observer measures the altitudes of Ax and A2 with 
a sextant. Then, the computed azimuth of Ai, and the difference between its 
measured and its computed altitudes, determine approximately a position 
circle Ci on the earth where the observer actually is located. Similarly, results 
for A2 determine a second position circle C2. Then, the observer’s position is 
found as an intersection of C2 and C2, cleverly approximated by means of the 
famous Sumner position lines.]
In any example of Problem III, the solution of the astronomical 
triangle ZPA is found to come under Case III of Chapter Thirteen. 
Methods involving use of haversines prove to be convenient.

Example 2. At a certain instant, an observer at 0 in latitude 44° 10', 
who knows his longitude, reads in a nautical almanac that star A has 
declination 22° 57' and hour angle 3 hours, 12 minutes east. Compute 
the altitude and azimuth of A.
Solution. 1. In AZP A of Figure 119,
PZ = 45° 50', PA = 90° - 22° 57' = 67° 3', ZZPA = 48° O', 
because 3 hours, 12 minutes equals 48°. We desire to find ZA, the coaltitude, 
and ZPZA, which equals the azimuth, measured by arc NET.
2. Letter AZPA with Z as B and P as C. Then, in the standard notation 
of Chapter Thirteen, we are given
a = 45° 50', b = 67° 3', 7 = 48° O'.
By the method of Section 129 on page 190, or Section 134 on page 196, we 
compute c = AZ = 44° 28' and 0 = Z PZA = 102° 20'. Hence, the desired 
altitude is 45° 32' and the azimuth is 102° 20'.
IV. To find the latitude of the observer if the altitude, hour angle, and 
declination of a celestial object are known.
The altitude of the object A in Problem IV would be measured with 
a sextant, while the hour angle and declination would be read from a 
nautical almanac, under the assumption that the observer knows his 
longitude. In Problem IV, the data for triangle ZPA consists of two 
sides and an angle opposite one of these sides, so that the solution comes 
under Case V of Chapter Thirteen. Sometimes, an ambiguity in a formal 
solution may be eliminated by a figure showing triangle ZPA.
Example 3. At 3:37 p.m. local solar time, when the sun’s declina- 
tion is — 16° 12', an observer measures the sun’s altitude as 22° 18' 
Find his latitude.
Solution. 1. In Figure 121, where A is the sun, ZA = 67° 42'; since the 
declination is negative, PA = 90° + 16° 12' = 106° 12'. Since 3 hours, 37 
minutes in angular measure equals 54° 15', hence ZZPA = 54° 15'. On 
solving AZPA by the method of Section 135 on page 197, we would obtain ZP, 
and then find the latitude L = 90° — ZP. If Section 135 has not been studied,:. 
AZP A could be solved by the method of Problem 9 on page 185, after con-;

structing an auxiliary great circle through A perpendicular to side ZP, to 
obtain related right triangles. On solving, we find PZ = 63° 43'. Hence, the 
latitude is 26° 17'.
Comment. In solving, an ambiguity about a second solution is eliminated 
because we must have PZ 90°, as seen in Figure 119, page 206.

X. AUXILIARY TABLES FOR ANGLES NEAR 0° OR 90°
Note 1, On account of large tabular differences, results obtained by interpolation in 
the preceding table are particularly inaccurate if we are determining log sin, log tan, 
or log cot for an angle near 0°, or log cos, log tan, or log cot for an angle near 90°. To 
avoid such interpolation in Table IX, we use Table Xa or Table X&.
Table Xb applies without interpolation if the angle is expressed to the nearest tenth 
of a minute, and if the angle differs from 0°, or 90°, by at most 2°. If angles are ex­
pressible to the nearest second, interpolation in Table Xb is sufficiently accurate except 
on the upper parts of pages 84 and 85.
Table Xa applies for the indicated values of the angle M in connection with the 
following formulas.*
A. Angle a near 0°. Let M be the number of minutes in a. Then, 
log sin a = S + log M; log tan a = T + log M. 
(1)
B. Angle a near 90°. Let M be the number of minutes in (90° — a). Then,
log cos a = S + log M\ 
log cot a = T + log M. 
(2)
In using (1) and (2), we find S and T from Table Xa below and log M from
Table VIII.
Note 2. If log cot a is desired in (A), or log tan a in (B), recall that tan a = —•
Table Xa. Auxiliary Table for S and T
To find S or T, subtract 10 from the given entry.
* For an explanation of these formulas, see page 176 of William L. Hart’s Plane Trigonometry, or 
page 176 of William L. Hart’s Plane and Spherical Trigonometry, or page 166 of William L. Hart’s 
College Trigonometry, all published by D. C. Heath and Company.
